Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hanafi Definition of the Quran
Definition of the Quran with clarification of Abu Hanifa's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the Quran and explain how Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation in salah does not contradict that definition.

Core Idea
The Quran is both wording and meaning, even though Abu Hanifa permitted Persian in salah.

This lesson unpacks the technical definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It then resolves a common misunderstanding: some scholars thought Abu Hanifa believed the Quran is only meaning because he allowed Persian recitation in prayer. The passage shows that his ruling was a special concession, not a change in the definition.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Break down each component: 'revelation to the Messenger', 'written in the mushafs', 'transmitted mutawatir', 'without doubt', 'both wording and meaning'. Note how each part excludes something (e.g., earlier scriptures, non-recited revelation, non-mutawatir readings).
The definition is designed to apply to the whole Quran and each part (verse).
2
Understand the exclusions
Identify what is excluded by each qualifier: 'revelation to the Messenger' excludes other divine books; 'written in the mushafs' excludes hadith qudsi and verses with abrogated recitation; 'mutawatir' excludes non-mutawatir readings.
The phrase 'bila shubhah' emphasizes the certainty of mutawatir transmission.
3
Examine the issue of nazm and the dispute
Note that the definition affirms the Quran is both nazm (wording) and ma'na (meaning). The passage then addresses the objection from Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation. It clarifies that he did not make nazm a rukn only for the specific context of prayer, not that nazm is unnecessary in general.
The Hanafi position is that nazm is a necessary pillar (rukn lazim) outside of prayer.
4
Apply the definition to the Persian recitation issue
Understand the practical difference: Abu Hanifa allowed Persian in prayer because the state of munajat (intimate conversation) might be disturbed by the eloquence of Arabic. But this does not imply that the Quran is only meaning; rather, it is a concession solely for the validity of prayer.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) only allowed it with an excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
تعريف الكتاب
الرسول ، المكتوب في المصاحف المنقول عنه نقلا متواتراً بلا شبهة وهو النظم والمعنى جميعاً
القرآن
في قول عامة العلماء، وهو الصحيح من مذهب أبي حنيفة حاله إلا أنه لم يجعل النظم ركناً لازماً في حق جواز الصلاة خاصة.
أي لكن أبا حنيفة
الآية،
الرسول: احتراز عن سائر الكتب السماوية، وقس عليه باقي القيود كما ستعلم فحينئذ هذا حقيقي لكتاب ابتداؤه من قوله: فالقرآن إلخ. المكتوب في المصاحف: احترز به عن الوحي الذي ليس بمتلو؛ لأنه منزل عليه صلى الله عليه وسلم ولكن لم يكتب في المصاحف، وعن منسوخ التلاوة، كقوله تعالى: "الشيخ والشيخة إذا زَنَيا فارْجُمُوهُما" واللام في المصاحف إن كان للجنس فيشمل جميع المصاحف، فيدخل حينئذ القراءة الشاذة والمشهورة، المكتوبة في مصاحف غير قراء السبعة، فيحصل الاحتراز بقوله: نقلا متواترا : لأن المشهورة الشاذة ليست منقولة بنقل متواتر وإن كان للعهد، ويكون المراد بها مصاحف القراء السبعة فقوله: "المنقول" "الخ" زائد لا فائدة فيه؛ إذ في مصاحف السبعة ليس إلا ما هو متواتر النقل، فتأمل. فإن قيل: قوله "المكتوب في المصاحف لايشمل إلا المنقوش، فخرج عن الحد ما هو ملفوظ ومحفوظ في صدور الرجال. أقول: لا ؛ لأن المراد به المثبت، فاللفظ مثبت حقيقة والمعنى تقديراً. بلا شبهة : أي في نقله تأكيد على مذهب الجمهور؛ لأن المتواتر لا يكون فيه شبهة عندهم، وعند الخصاف احتراز عن المشهور؛ لأن المشهور عنده قسم من المتواتر ولكنه يورث الشبهة.
ثم اعلم أن الكتاب والقرآن عند أرباب الأصول يطلق على المجموع وعلى كل جزء منه؛ لأن بحثهم عنه من حيث إنه دليل على الحكم وذلك آية آية فلذا أخذوا في تعريفه ما يصدق على الكل وعلى كل جزء منه، كالإنزال والكتابة في المصاحف والنقل، كما أخذ المصنف وبعضهم الإنزال والإعجاز فقط؛ لأن النقل والكتابة ليسا من لوازم القرآن؛ لتحققه بغيرهما في زمن النبي عليه السلام فهذا التعريف المذكور في المتن تعريف يصدق على الكل وعلى كل جزء ولما فرغ عن تعريف القرآن شرع في تقسيمه ولكن مهد لتقسيمه قوله : "وهو " إلخ. النظم: أي اللفظ، وإنما عبّر عن النظم رعاية لجانب الأدب؛ إذ النظم حقيقة في جمع اللؤلؤ في السلك، ومنه نظم الشعر، واللفظ حقيقة في الرمي. والمعنى جميعا لا أنه اسم للنظم فقط كما يتوهم عن تعريفه بالإنزال والكتابة والنقل، ولا أنه اسم للمعنى فقط،
كما يتوهم من تجويز أبي حنيفة بالله للقراءة الفارسية في الصلاة بغير عذر مع أن قراءة القرآن فيها فرض.
عامة العلماء ولما كان يتوهم من تجويز أبي حنيفة أن مذهبه أنه المعنى فقط دفع توهمه أولا. وهو الصحيح إلخ ثم دفع استدلال المتوهم ثانيا: إلا أنه إلخ. جواز الصلاة خاصة وأما في غير جواز الصلاة فالنظم ركن لازم كالمعنى حتى يجوز للجنب والحائض قراءة آية من القرآن بالفارسية؛ لأنه ليس بقرآن لعدم النظم، وإليه يشير قوله: "خاصة"، فالحاصل لا يلزم من تجويز أبي حنيفة أن مذهبه أنه المعنى فقط؛ لأن ما جوزه أبو حنيفة هو
في حق جواز الصلاة خاصة لكون حالة الصلاة حالة المناجاة الله تعالى، ونظم القرآن بليغ عذب، فلعله يشتغل
The Book of Allah Almighty. Definition of the Book: revelation to the Messenger, written in the mushafs, transmitted from him with mutawatir transmission without doubt, and it is both the wording and meaning together. That is the Quran according to the general scholars, and it is the correct view of Abu Hanifa's school, except that he did not make the wording an essential pillar with regard to the permissibility of prayer specifically. i.e. But Abu Hanifa made the wording not a necessary pillar solely for the permissibility of prayer. 'The revelation to the Messenger' excludes other divine books; analogize the rest of the conditions as you will learn. So this is a real definition of the Book starting from his statement: 'the Quran' etc. 'Written in the mushafs' excludes revelation that is not recited; because it was revealed to him but not written in the mushafs, and abrogated recitation like His statement: 'The old man and the old woman, when they commit fornication, stone them.' The lam in 'mushafs' if for genus includes all mushafs, thus including irregular and famous readings written in mushafs of other than the seven reciters, so exclusion occurs by his statement: 'mutawatir transmission' because irregular famous readings are not transmitted with mutawatir transmission, but if the lam is for covenant, referring to mushafs of the seven reciters, then his statement 'transmitted' etc. is superfluous; because in the mushafs of the seven there is only what is mutawatir. Reflect. If it is said: His statement 'written in the mushafs' only includes inscribed, so what is spoken and preserved in the hearts of men is excluded from the definition. I say: No; because what is meant is established, so the utterance is established in reality and the meaning in estimation. 'Without doubt' means in its transmission, an emphasis according to the majority; because the mutawatir has no doubt according to them, and according to al-Khassaf it excludes the mashhur; because the mashhur according to him is a part of the mutawatir but it engenders doubt. Then know that 'Kitab' and 'Quran' according to usul scholars are applied to the whole and to each part; because their investigation of it is as a proof of the ruling, and that is verse by verse. So they included in its definition what applies to the whole and to each part, like revelation, writing in mushafs, and transmission, as the author took, and some took only revelation and inimitability; because transmission and writing are not necessary attributes of the Quran, as it existed without them in the time of the Prophet. So this definition mentioned in the text applies to the whole and each part. When he finished defining the Quran he began its division, but he prepared for its division by his statement: 'and it is' etc. 'Al-nazm' means the wording, and he expressed it as nazm out of literary propriety; because nazm literally means stringing pearls, and from it comes 'nazm al-shi'r' (poetry), while 'lafz' literally means throwing. 'And meaning together' so that it is not a name for the wording only as might be imagined from its definition by revelation, writing, and transmission, nor a name for meaning only, as might be imagined from Abu Hanifa's permissibility of reading the Quran in Persian in prayer without excuse, while reciting the Quran therein is obligatory. 'General scholars' and when it was imagined from Abu Hanifa's permissibility that his school is that it is only meaning, he first refuted that misapprehension: 'and it is correct' etc. then he refuted the objector's proof second: 'except that' etc. 'Permissibility of prayer specifically' and as for other than permissibility of prayer, the wording is a necessary pillar like the meaning, so that it is not permissible for a junub or menstruating woman to recite a verse of the Quran in Persian; because it is not Quran due to lack of nazm, and his statement 'specifically' indicates this. So the conclusion: the permissibility granted by Abu Hanifa does not imply that his school is that it is only meaning; because what he permitted is specifically regarding the permissibility of prayer, since the state of prayer is a state of intimate conversation with Allah Almighty, and the nazm of the Quran is eloquent and sweet, perhaps it would distract.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an extended definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, followed by a defense of Imam Abu Hanifa's position. The definition given is: 'The Book of Allah is the revelation sent down to the Messenger, written in the mushafs, transmitted from him with a mutawatir transmission without any doubt, and it is both the nazm (wording) and the meaning together.' This is stated to be the position of the generality of scholars and the correct view in Abu Hanifa's school. However, the author notes that Abu Hanifa did not treat the nazm as a constitutive pillar (rukn) in the specific context of the permissibility of prayer—meaning he allowed recitation in Persian in salah under certain conditions. The commentary then unpacks each part of the definition: 'revelation to the Messenger' excludes earlier scriptures; 'written in the mushafs' excludes non-recited revelation (like hadith qudsi) and verses with abrogated recitation (like the stoning verse); 'mutawatir transmission' ensures the text is definitively established. A significant portion of the passage is devoted to clarifying that Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation in prayer does not mean he believed the Quran is only meaning. The author explains that Abu Hanifa considered nazm a necessary pillar outside of prayer, and only set it aside in prayer due to the nature of munajat (intimate conversation) where eloquent Arabic might distract. Thus, the Quran in Hanafi usul is both lafz and ma'na, and the exception is limited to salah.

Key Terms
القرآن (al-Qur'an): The revealed Book sent to the Prophet, written in mushafs, transmitted mutawatir, consisting of both wording and meaning.
النظم (al-nazm): The wording or expression of the Quran
متواتر (mutawatir): Transmission by a large number that precludes doubt and fabrication

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul definition in this passage, what does 'without doubt' (بلا شبهة) primarily exclude?
A) Non-recited revelation
B) Verses with abrogated recitation
C) Readings that are not transmitted mutawatir
D) The Quran memorized in hearts

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of the Quran affirms both wording and meaning; Abu Hanifa's limited concession in prayer does not change this.

The Vow/Oath Dichotomy: Literal Meaning and Necessary Consequence
Analyzes whether 'الله علي' is a vow or oath and establishes the usul rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'الله علي' is not a prohibited combination of literal and metaphorical meanings, and apply the principle that when literal meaning is possible, metaphorical meaning is not intended.

Core Idea
A single formulation cannot be both literal and metaphorical; a metaphorical meaning may arise from its necessary consequence.

This passage tackles a subtle usul issue: does the statement 'الله علي' combine both literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) meanings? The questioner thought such a combination is prohibited. The commentator clarifies that the literal meaning comes from the formulation itself, while the metaphorical meaning arises from the necessary consequence of the vow. A critique follows about the role of intention, and Shams al-A'immah resolves it by parsing the phrase into two separate formulations. The lesson also reinforces the foundational usul rule that literal meaning takes precedence over metaphorical when possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine whether the statement 'الله علي' is a prohibited combination of literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) meanings.
2
Understand the author's argument
The author argues that the formulation is a vow literally (صيغة نذر), and the oath is not from the same word but from the necessary consequence (موجب) of the vow: making permissible obligatory resembles making permissible forbidden, which the Quran calls an oath.
Quranic proof from At-Tahrim 66:1-2 is cited.
3
Analyze the critique
A critic objects: if the oath is a necessary consequence, it should not require intention, just as buying a relative automatically causes emancipation. But the Hanafi school requires intention for an oath.
This highlights the tension between consequence and intention.
4
Examine Shams al-A'immah's resolution
Shams al-A'immah argues that 'لله' is an oath formulation (like 'والله') and 'علي' is a vow formulation, so the two meanings come from two separate words, not one. Thus no prohibited combination occurs.
This avoids the intention requirement issue because the oath is explicit.
5
Apply the general usul principle
The passage concludes with the rule: 'When it is possible to use the word in its literal meaning, the metaphorical meaning is not intended.' This underpins the entire analysis.
This principle is central to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ عن الجواب عن مسألة: "لايضع قدمه في دار "فلان" شرع في الجواب عن مسألة النذر واليمين. فليس بجمع: يعني ليس في تلك المسئلة جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز كما فهم السائل؛ لأن المراد من الجمع الممتنع أن يكون اجتماعهما من صيغة واحدة، وهنا ليس كذلك بل هو أي الكلام المذكور نذر بصيغته؛ لأن قوله: "الله علي" صيغة نذر وضع له في الشرع، وهو معناه الموضوع له، فلم يثبت من الصيغة إلا المعنى الحقيقي وهو النذر، لا المجازي وهو اليمين، فلم يلزم الجمع بينهما من صيغته لكن هذا الكلام يمين بموجبه . وهو الإيجاب: إذ المقصود من صيغة النذر إيجاب المنذور الذي كان مباح الترك قبله؛ إذ لو كان واجباً قبله لم يصح النذر على ما عرف، فلمّا صار واجباً من النذر كان يمينا. يصلح يمينا : لأن الشيء إذا صار واجباً كان تركه حراماً، وقد كان قبل ذلك مباحاً، فصار إيجاب المباح كتحريم المباح، وتحريم المباح أي الحلال يمين؛ لأن النبي لو كان حرم على نفسه مارية أو العسل ، قال : ولِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ كله (التحريم:۱)، فسمّى الله ذلك يميناً. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ﴾ (التحريم: ۲)، فثبت أن اليمين إنما حصلت من موجب الكلام لا هي مراد بطريق المجاز. بصيغته إذ قوله : "اشتريت" إنما وضع شرعاً لإفادة الملك بموجبه: إذ يحصل من الشراء التملك، والملك في القريب يوجب العتق شرعاً، فكان الشراء إعتاقاً بواسطة حكمه لا بصيغته أقول: هذا الجواب لا يستقيم؛ لأن اليمين لو كانت موجب الكلام لثبتت بدون النية أيضاً؛ لأن موجب الشيء لايحتاج إلى النية، كشراء القريب يثبت العتق فيه بغير النية أيضاً، والحال أنكم تشترطون النية لثبوت اليمين؟ اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنها كالحقيقة المحجورة، فلذا يحتاج إلى النية، وقد تكلّف الشراح في الجواب، وأجاب شمس الأئمة أن قوله: "لله" بمعنى "والله" صيغة يمين. وقوله: "علي" صيغة نذر، فلا يجتمعان في لفظ واحد، فلم يحصل الجمع في كلمة واحدة في كلمتين، وذلك غير مستبعد فتأمل. ولما فرغ عن الأجوبة شرع في مسألة أخرى. هذا الباب: أي باب الحقيقة والمجاز. متى أمكن إلخ يعني مادام يمكن حمل اللفظ على معناه الحقيقي لايراد معناه المجازي.
When he finished with the answer to the issue: 'He does not place his foot in the house of so-and-so', he began the answer to the issue of vow and oath. So it is not combination: meaning there is no combination of literal and metaphorical in that issue as the questioner understood; because what is meant by the prohibited combination is that both [meanings] come from a single formulation. Here it is not like that, rather the mentioned speech is a vow by its formulation, because his saying 'الله علي' is the formulation of a vow placed for it in the Law, and that is its assigned meaning. So the formulation only establishes the literal meaning, which is the vow, not the metaphorical which is the oath. So combination between them is not necessary from its formulation. However, this speech is an oath by its necessary consequence, which is obligation: because the purpose of the vow formulation is to make obligatory the thing vowed which was permissible to abandon before; for if it were obligatory before, the vow would not be valid as is known. So when it becomes obligatory through the vow, it becomes an oath. It is suitable as an oath because when something becomes obligatory, its abandonment becomes forbidden, while it was permissible before. So making permissible obligatory becomes like making permissible forbidden. And making permissible forbidden is an oath because the Prophet (ﷺ) when he forbade himself Māriyah or honey, Allah said: 'Why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' (At-Tahrim:1), and Allah called that an oath. Allah said: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (At-Tahrim:2). So it is established that the oath came from the necessary consequence of the speech, not that it is intended metaphorically. By its formulation: [The author says] his saying 'I bought' is only placed in the Law to effect ownership by its necessary consequence: because from purchase comes ownership, and ownership of a relative necessitates emancipation legally. So purchase is emancipation by way of its ruling, not by its formulation. I say: This answer is not sound because if the oath were the necessary consequence of the speech, it would be established without intention as well; because the necessary consequence of a thing does not require intention, like the purchase of a relative establishes emancipation without intention as well. However, you require intention for establishing the oath. Unless it is said that it is like a restricted literal meaning, so it requires intention. The commentators have made efforts in the answer, and Shams al-A'immah answered that his saying 'لله' is in the meaning of 'والله', a formulation of oath, and his saying 'علي' is a formulation of vow. So they do not combine in a single word, so combination did not occur in one word but in two words, and that is not far-fetched. So ponder. When he finished the answers, he began another issue. 'This chapter' meaning the chapter of literal and metaphorical. 'When possible etc.' means as long as it is possible to carry the word upon its literal meaning, its metaphorical meaning is not intended.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary by addressing a second issue: whether the statement 'الله علي' combines literal and metaphorical meanings. The questioner thought that since a vow (نذر) and an oath (يمين) are both possible meanings, it would be a prohibited combination. The author explains that the formulation 'الله علي' is a vow by its literal meaning (صيغة نذر). The metaphorical meaning (oath) arises only from the necessary consequence (موجب) of the vow—making permissible things obligatory, which is similar to making permissible things forbidden. The author supports this with Quranic verses from Surat At-Tahrim (1-2), where Allah calls prohibiting permissible things an oath. However, a critique is raised: if the oath is a necessary consequence, it should be established without intention, but the Hanafi school requires intention for oaths. The author mentions Shams al-A'immah's response that 'الله' is an oath formulation (والله) and 'علي' is a vow formulation, so they are two separate words, not a single ambiguous word. The passage concludes with the general usul principle: when a literal meaning is possible, the metaphorical meaning is not used.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow: a commitment to perform an act of worship that was previously permissible to omit. Here, the formulation 'الله علي' is a vow.
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath: a declaration that invokes Allah's name to affirm or promise something. In this passage, it arises from the necessary consequence of a vow (making permissible obligatory).
الموجب (al-mūjib): The necessary consequence of a statement. Here, the obligation created by the vow is the 'موجب' that turns the vow into an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the statement 'الله علي' not considered a prohibited combination of literal and metaphorical meanings according to the text?
A) Because the literal meaning (vow) and metaphorical meaning (oath) come from two separate words.
B) Because the metaphorical meaning is derived from the necessary consequence, not directly from the word.
C) Because the Hanafi school prohibits combining meanings only in oaths.
D) Because the metaphorical meaning requires intention and is not automatically present.

Key Takeaway
When a literal meaning is possible, the metaphorical meaning is not intended; but a metaphorical meaning may arise from the legal consequence of a literal statement.

Priority of Literal vs. Common Metaphorical Meaning in Oaths: The Principle of Metaphor as Substitute
Comparing literal and common metaphorical meaning in oaths and explaining the principle of metaphor as substitute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi dispute regarding the priority of literal vs. common metaphorical meaning in oaths and identify the underlying principle of metaphor as substitute (khalaf) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
When literal and metaphorical meanings conflict, Abu Hanifa gives priority to the literal because metaphor is a substitute in speech, while the two companions prefer the common metaphorical meaning because metaphor is a substitute in ruling.

This lesson explores a key rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when a word has both a literal meaning (ḥaqīqah) and a more common metaphorical meaning (majāz muta‘ārif), which takes precedence in interpreting oaths? The answer depends on a deeper principle: the nature of metaphor as a substitute for the literal. Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on whether metaphor is a substitute in speech (khalaf fī al-takallum) or in ruling (khalaf fī al-ḥukm), leading to different outcomes in cases like swearing not to eat wheat or drink from a river.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The passage deals with a word that has both a literal meaning that is still used and a metaphorical meaning that is more common. Identify the two meanings in the given examples.
Literal meaning: still practiced in some contexts. Metaphorical meaning: more frequent in everyday usage.
2
Identify the dispute
State the positions: Abu Hanifa gives priority to the literal meaning; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give priority to the common metaphorical meaning.
In one narration of the matn, it says 'with the general metaphorical meaning is preferable'.
3
Examine the examples
Work through the oath examples: 'not to eat from this wheat' and 'not to drink from this Euphrates'. Determine what breaks the oath according to each view.
For wheat: literal = grain; metaphorical = bread. For Euphrates: literal = lapping; metaphorical = drinking from cup or scoop.
4
Link to the principle of khalaf
Understand that the dispute over priority is rooted in a deeper disagreement about the nature of metaphor as a substitute for the literal. Abu Hanifa sees it as substitute in speech; the two as substitute in ruling.
Substitute in speech: linguistic soundness suffices. Substitute in ruling: literal meaning must be possible.
5
Apply the principle to the emancipation example
Analyze the case of a man saying to his older slave 'This is my son' intending freedom. According to Abu Hanifa, the speech is valid and the slave is freed because the utterance is linguistically sound. According to the two, it is void because the literal meaning (sonship) is impossible.
This example illustrates the practical consequence of the different views on khalaf.

فإن كان اللفظ له حقيقة مستعملة ومجاز متعارف أي غالب الاستعمال من الحقيقة، أو غالب منها في الفهم اللفظ، فحينئذ عند أبي حنيفة العمل بالحقيقة أولى، وعندهما العمل بالمجاز أولى. وفي رواية المتن بعموم المجاز أولى، كما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة، فإن حقيقته أن يأكل من عين الحنطة، وهذا المعنى الحقيقي مستعمل في العرف كأنها تغلى وتقلى وتوكل قضماً، ولكن المعنى المجازي هو أكل الخبز المتخذ منها غالب العربية والترجمة لإيجاب الحقيقة: أي: وإن لم يكن إفادة هذا الكلام للمعنى الحقيقي له. هذا ابني: فقوله: "هذا ابني" مراداً به الحرية خلف عن "هذا ابني" مراداً به البنوة، فيعتق العبد عنده؛ لأنه قد وجد ما يُصحح الاستعارة بهذا الكلام، وهو استقامة الأصل من حيث العربية؛ لأن هذا الكلام صحيح بعبارته من حيث كونه مبتدأ وخبراً موضوعاً لإثبات الحكم، وقد تعذر العمل بحقيقته؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الولد من والده فتعين المجاز فيراد به العتق بطريق ذكر الملزوم وإرادة اللازم، وعندهما المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة في الحكم، أي حكم "هذا ابني" مراداً به الحرية خلف عن حكمه مراداً به البنوة، فينبغي أن يكون الأصل في موضعه صحيحاً موجباً للحكم على الاحتمال ولكن تعذر العمل به لعارض، فيصار إلى المجاز، فعندهما هذا الكلام لغو لا يعتق به العبد ؛ لأن إمكان المعنى الحقيقي الذي شرط لصحة المجاز لا يوجد في هذا الكلام؛ لأن الأكبر سناً لا يمكن أن يكون ابنا للأصغر، فلا يحمل على المجاز الذي هو العتق ولما ثبت أن المجاز عنده خلف في التكلم كما مرّ وعندهما في الحكم كما سيجئ في كلام المصنف فاعتبر الخ. في التكلم بأن جعل التكلم بالحقيقة عند إمكان العمل بها راجحاً على التكلم بالمجاز؛ إذ العبرة عنده : الكرع: بأن يتناول الماء بفيه من موضع الماء الكرع بفتحتين، آب هدهان خوردن. للتكلم
If the word has a literal meaning that is used (in practice) and a common metaphorical meaning (i.e., more frequent in usage than the literal, or more dominant in understanding the word), then according to Abu Hanifa acting upon the literal is preferable, and according to the two acting upon the metaphorical is preferable. In one narration of the text (matn) 'with the general metaphorical meaning is preferable', as if he swore not to eat from this wheat, then its literal meaning is to eat from the actual wheat grain, and this literal meaning is used in custom such as when it is boiled, fried, or eaten raw, but the metaphorical meaning is eating bread made from it, which is the common usage in custom. Thus according to him he does not break his oath by eating other than the actual wheat grain, and according to the two he breaks his oath by eating bread, or by eating bread and the actual wheat grain, by way of general metaphorical meaning. As for sawiq (ground wheat), it is in custom another type so it is not considered, nor does it enter under the general metaphorical meaning. This goes back to a principle: that metaphor is a substitute for the literal in speech according to Abu Hanifa, such that borrowing (isti'ara) by it is valid in his view even if it does not necessitate the literal meaning, as in his statement to his slave who is older than him: 'This is my son.' So he considered the preponderance in speech (i.e., the slave is the speaker according to Abu Hanifa). Similarly, if he swears not to drink from this Euphrates, its literal meaning is to drink from the Euphrates by lapping (kur'), and this literal meaning is used as you see among desert dwellers, but the metaphorical meaning is drinking by scooping or from a vessel, which is common usage. So according to him he breaks his oath only by lapping, and according to the two by vessel and scooping by way of metaphor, or by both and by lapping together by way of general metaphor. This difference mentioned goes back to another principle they differ on. That metaphor: there is no dispute that metaphor is a branch and substitute for the literal in the sense that the literal is the preponderant origin given priority, and one only resorts to metaphor when the literal is impossible. Also there is no dispute that in the substitute, the origin must be conceivable even if not present due to an impediment. But the dispute is in the aspect of substitute. According to him (Abu Hanifa), metaphor is a substitute in speech, so that the validity of speech suffices: the utterance is sound from the perspective of Arabic and translation (interpretation) even if the literal meaning does not exist. The borrowing by it is valid if the utterance is sound in Arabic and translation for necessitating the literal meaning? i.e., even if it does not convey the literal meaning. 'This is my son' intending freedom is a substitute for 'this is my son' intending sonship, so the slave is freed in his view because there exists that which makes the borrowing valid in this speech, i.e., the soundness of the origin from the perspective of Arabic; for this speech is sound in its wording as a subject and predicate established for proving a legal ruling, and acting upon its literal meaning is impossible because it is impossible for the older to be the son of the younger, so metaphor is determined meaning freedom by way of mentioning the necessary concomitant and intending the necessary. According to the two, metaphor is a substitute for the literal in ruling, i.e., the ruling of 'this is my son' intending freedom is a substitute for its ruling intending sonship. Therefore the origin must in its place be sound, necessitating the ruling upon probability, but acting upon it is impossible due to an impediment, so one resorts to metaphor. Thus in their view this speech is void (laghw) and the slave is not freed because the possibility of the literal meaning, which is a condition for the validity of metaphor, does not exist in this speech because the older cannot be the son of the younger, so it is not taken as metaphor for freedom. And since it is established that metaphor in his view is a substitute in speech as mentioned, and in their view in ruling as will come in the author's words, consider etc. In speech, he made the speaking of the literal when possible preponderant over the speaking of metaphor; because the criterion in his view is: al-kur': to take water into the mouth from the water source, kur' is with two fatha, drinking water with the mouth.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on rules for interpreting oaths. It presents the scenario where the literal meaning is still used in custom but the metaphorical meaning is more common. For example, swearing not to eat 'this wheat'. Literally it means eating the grain itself, but metaphorically it commonly means eating bread made from it. Abu Hanifa says the literal meaning takes precedence, so the oath is only broken by eating the actual grain. The two companions say the common metaphorical meaning takes precedence, so the oath is broken by eating bread. The passage then goes to the underlying principle: metaphor (مجاز) is a substitute for the literal (حقيقة). The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two is about the aspect of this substitution. For Abu Hanifa, metaphor is a substitute in speech (خلف في التكلم), meaning that as long as the utterance is linguistically sound (grammatically correct and capable of meaning), it is valid even if the literal meaning is impossible. He illustrates with a man saying to his slave who is older than him, 'This is my son', meaning 'This is free' (as a declaration of emancipation). Since it is impossible for the older to be the son of the younger, the literal meaning is impossible, but the utterance is still valid as a metaphor for freedom because the speech is sound in Arabic. Thus the slave is freed. For the two companions, metaphor is a substitute in ruling (خلف في الحكم), meaning that the literal meaning must be possible in itself (i.e., the statement could be true) for the metaphor to be valid; if the literal is impossible (like an older being son of a younger), then the metaphor fails and the speech is considered void (لغو). Therefore, in the 'this is my son' example, they do not free the slave. This difference also explains the previous dispute: Abu Hanifa sees the metaphorical meaning as a substitute in speech, so he sticks to the literal when possible; the two see it as substitute in ruling, so they prefer the common metaphorical meaning. The passage also includes a note on 'al-kur'' meaning to drink directly from the water source, and a Persian gloss.

Key Terms
مجاز متعارف (majāz muta‘ārif): A metaphorical meaning that is commonly used and more frequent than the literal meaning.
خلف في التكلم (khalaf fī al-takallum): Metaphor is a substitute in speech; the validity of the utterance as Arabic speech is sufficient.
خلف في الحكم (khalaf fī al-ḥukm): Metaphor is a substitute in ruling; the literal meaning must be possible for the metaphor to be valid.
الكرع (al-kur‘): Drinking water directly by taking it into the mouth from the source, i.e., lapping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of swearing not to drink from the Euphrates, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, is the oath broken by drinking from a cup?
A) Yes, because the metaphorical meaning includes cup drinking.
B) No, only lapping breaks it.
C) No, because the literal meaning is impossible.
D) Yes, because the river water is not cup water.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's dispute on whether metaphor is a substitute in speech or in ruling determines the priority of literal vs. metaphorical meaning in oaths and the validity of metaphorical declarations like 'this is my son' for emancipation.

Explicit and Implicit Speech and Their Rulings
Defines explicit and implicit speech with legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish explicit (sarih) from implicit (kinaya) speech and apply the Hanafi ruling that explicit speech produces a legal effect without intention, while implicit speech requires intention or context.

Core Idea
Explicit speech binds by its words; implicit speech binds only by intention.

This lesson introduces two critical categories of speech in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: sarih (explicit) and kinaya (implicit). These categories determine when a legal effect, such as divorce, takes effect judicially regardless of the speaker's intent. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying rulings in contracts and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terminology
Recognize the Arabic terms sarih and kinaya and their linguistic roots. Sarih means clear and unambiguous; kinaya means hidden or indirect.
The passage defines sarih as 'that whose meaning appears with clear manifestation'.
2
Understand the legal effect of explicit speech
Note that sarih establishes the ruling without need for intent. The legal effect attaches to the words themselves, and they stand in place of the meaning, so the speaker is dispensed from having to intend the meaning.
Even if the speaker accidentally utters sarih words, the effect occurs judicially (qada'an), though religiously (diyanatan) intention matters.
3
Understand the legal effect of implicit speech
Kinaya does not obligate a ruling unless the speaker intends the meaning or there is a contextual indication (e.g., anger) that reveals the intended meaning.
The passage uses the example of 'You are ba'in' which only causes divorce if the speaker intended divorce or the context shows it.
4
Analyse the boundary between sarih and kinaya
Metaphorical speech (majaz) that becomes conventionally recognized (mut‘āraf) is treated as sarih, because the meaning is no longer hidden. Before that, it remains kinaya.
The passage states: 'when it becomes conventionally recognized, it is explicit.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصريح والكناية وحكمهما وأما الصريح فمثل قوله بعتُ واشتريت ووهبت وحكمه تعلق الحكم بعين الكلام، وقيامه مقام معناه حتى استغني عن العزيمة؛ لأنه ظاهر المراد. وحكم الكناية أنه لا يجب العمل به إلا بالنية؛ لأنه أي النية مستتر المراد، وذلك مثل المجاز قبل أن يصير متعارفا. الصريح: فما ظهر المراد به ظهوراً بينا حقيقة كان أو مجازا، وهو فعيل بمعنى فاعل من صَرَحَ يَصْرحُ صرا صراحةً وصروحة إذا انكشف وأخلص، فسمّي به؛ لانكشافه وخلوصه عن محتملاته، والشيخ لم يفسره لحصول المقصود ببيان النظائر، فأعرض مقام معناه : يعني حكم الصريح أن يتعلق الحكم بنفس الكلام ويقوم ذلك الكلام مقام معناه حتى استغني إلخ أي لا يحتاج إلى أن ينوي المتكلم، ويقصد هذا المعنى من كلامه فيثبت بغير قصد وعزيمة، حتى لو قصد أن يقول : الحمد الله، فجرى على لسانه أنت طالق، يقع الطلاق بغير قصده. ظاهر المراد: فلا يحتاج إلى النية والقصد قضاء، وأما ديانةً فله ما نوى بالنية أي بنية المتكلم أو ما يقوم مقامها كدلالة الحال مستتر المراد: فكان في ثبوت المراد تردّد، فلا يوجب الحكم ما لم يزل ذلك الاستتار والتردد بالنية أو ما يقوم مقامها، فلا يقع الطلاق بقوله: "أنت بائن" ما لم ينوي الطلاق، أو لم يكن شيء قائماً مقامها، كدلالة حالة الغضب أو مذاكرة الطلاق متعارفاً: فالمجاز متى لا يتعارف بين الناس بعد كناية؛ لأن المتكلم ستر المراد به عن السامع، فصار المراد في حقه في حيّز التردد، فكان كناية، بخلاف ما إذا صار متعارفاً فإنه صريح.
Explicit and implicit speech and their rulings. As for explicit speech, it is like one's statement: 'I sold,' 'I bought,' 'I gifted.' Its ruling is that the legal effect is attached to the very words, and they stand in place of their meaning so that one is dispensed from intent, because it is apparent in meaning. The ruling of implicit speech is that it is not obligatory to act upon it except with intention, because the intended meaning is hidden. That is like metaphorical speech before it becomes conventionally recognized. Explicit speech: that whose meaning appears with clear manifestation, whether literal or metaphorical. It is the pattern fa‘īl meaning fā‘il, from ṣaraḥa yaṣraḥu ṣaran ṣarāḥatan wa ṣurūḥatan when it becomes clear and pure. It is named thus due to its clarity and purity from possibilities. The shaykh did not define it because the purpose is achieved by explaining its counterparts, so he turned away from its standing in meaning. That is, the ruling of explicit speech is that the legal effect is attached to the words themselves, and those words stand in place of their meaning so that one is dispensed from intent, etc. That is, there is no need for the speaker to intend and purpose this meaning from his speech; it is established without intent and resolve. Even if he intended to say 'Praise be to Allah' but his tongue uttered 'You are divorced,' the divorce occurs without his intent. 'Apparent in meaning': so there is no need for intention and purpose in the judicial ruling. As for the religious (diyāna), he has what he intended. 'Intention' means the intention of the speaker or what stands in its place like the indication of the state. 'Hidden meaning': so there is hesitation in establishing the meaning, so it does not obligate a ruling until that concealment and hesitation are removed by intention or what stands in its place. Thus divorce does not occur by his saying 'You are ba'in' unless he intends divorce, or there is something that stands in its place, like the indication of a state of anger or discussion of divorce. 'Conventionally recognized': when metaphorical speech becomes conventionally recognized among people, it is no longer implicit; because the speaker concealed the meaning from the listener, so the meaning with respect to him is in a state of hesitation, so it is implicit. But when it becomes conventionally recognized, it is explicit.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the two categories of speech: explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinaya). Explicit speech is clear in its intended meaning, such as 'I sold' or 'I divorced,' and its legal effect takes effect without the speaker's intent. For example, if one accidentally says 'You are divorced' intending to say 'Praise be to Allah,' the divorce still occurs judicially (qada’an). However, religiously (diyanatan), the speaker's intention is considered. Implicit speech is ambiguous, such as 'You are ba'in' (a word that can imply divorce but is not explicit). It does not produce a legal effect unless the speaker intends divorce or there is a contextual indication like anger. The passage also clarifies that metaphorical speech that becomes conventional (mut‘āraf) is treated as explicit, because the meaning is no longer hidden. The commentary follows the author's sequence: first defining the terms, then giving their rulings, and finally explaining the reasoning.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit speech whose meaning is clear and not ambiguous, whether literal or metaphorical.
كناية (kinaya): Implicit speech whose intended meaning is hidden and requires intention or contextual indication to be understood.
ديانةً (diyanatan): In the religious sense, considering the person's inner intention and sincerity before Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man intending to say 'Alhamdulillah' accidentally says 'You are divorced' to his wife, what is the judicial ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Divorce does not occur because he did not intend it.
B) Divorce occurs because the words are explicit and clear.
C) Divorce occurs only if the wife was present.
D) It depends on the state of anger.

Key Takeaway
Explicit speech (sarih) is self-sufficient in establishing a legal ruling; implicit speech (kinaya) requires intention or contextual indication.

Ta'liq vs Khiyar al-Shart: Where Does the Condition Enter?
Explaining the distinction between khiyar al-shart in sale and ta'liq in talaq/itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'liq in talaq/itq and khiyar al-shart in sale, explaining how the condition attaches to the cause versus the ruling using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Ta'liq annihilates the cause until the condition; khiyar al-shart leaves the cause intact and affects the ruling.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text explains a key distinction: when a condition is attached to an act, does it affect the cause (sabab) or the ruling (hukm)? In sale, the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) enters upon the ruling, leaving the sale contract intact. In divorce and emancipation, the condition enters upon the cause itself, annihilating it until the condition occurs. This difference is rooted in the nature of each act and is critical for correct legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
First, consider khiyar al-shart in sale: the condition enters on the ruling (hukm) of the sale, not the cause (the contract). Evidence: if one swears not to sell, then sells with an option, he breaks his oath—the sale is considered to have occurred. Second, consider ta'liq in talaq and itq: the condition enters on the cause itself. For example, saying 'if I marry you, you are divorced' suspends the divorce until marriage.
The key is whether the condition nullifies the cause or just the effect.
2
Understand the Rationale for the Difference
Sale is an act of affirmation (ithbat) that can potentially become prohibited (e.g., leading to gambling). The Lawgiver permitted sale with an option condition as an exception (istihsan) to prevent loss (ghubn). This exception is limited, so the condition only affects the ruling. Talaq and itq are not inherently prohibited; they can bear suspension, so the condition can affect the cause directly.
The permissibility of ta'liq is the default, not an exception.
3
Analyze the Opposing Qiyas
Some jurists might analogize ta'liq to khiyar al-shart, arguing that in both cases the condition should affect only the ruling. The Hanafi response: this is 'qiyas ma' al-fariq' (analogy with a difference) because the underlying rationale differs. Sale needs the condition on the ruling due to necessity; talaq and itq have no such necessity.
Identifying the 'fariq' (difference) is a key usul skill.
4
Apply the Principle to a New Case
Consider a man who says, 'If I acquire a slave, then he is free.' According to Hanafi ta'liq, the emancipation is suspended: the cause (the act of itq) is annihilated until ownership occurs. Only when he acquires ownership does the cause take effect and the slave becomes free.
This is a direct application of the rule 'ta'liq enters on the cause.'
5
Note the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
The distinction affects when acts are considered to have occurred. In sale with option, the sale is effective immediately but can be rescinded. In ta'liq of talaq, the divorce does not occur until the condition is fulfilled. This impacts issues like inheritance, iddah, and maintenance.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجوه الفاسدة
كتاب الله تعالى لأن الخيار داخل على الحكم دون السبب، ولهذا لو حلف: لا يبيع، فباع بشرط [أي خيار الشرط في البيع] فقال: والله الخيار يحنث، وإذا ثبت أن التعليق تصرف في السبب بإعدامه إلى زمان وجود الشرط، لا في أحكامه صحّ تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك، وبطل التكفير بالمال قبل الحنث، وفرقه بين المالي والبدني ساقط؛ لأن حق الله تعالى في المالي فعل الأداء، والمال آلة وإنما يُقصد عين المال في حقوق العباد.
كشرط الخيار في البيع يدخل على الحكم دون السبب. وتقرير الجواب: هذا أي دخول الشرط في العتاق والطلاق على السبب دون الحكم، بخلاف خيار الشرط في البيع يدخل على الحكم دون السبب وهو البيع؛ لأن البيع يحتمل الحظر؛ لأنه من قبيل الإثبات وهو غير محتمل للحظر؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى القمار الذي هو حرام، وفي جعله متعلقاً بالشرط حظر تامّ فكان القياس أن لا يجوز البيع مع الشرط كما لا يجوز مع سائر الشروط، لكن الشرع جوّز ذلك ضرورة دفع الغبن، فيتقدر بقدرها، وهي تندفع بجعله متعلقاً بالحكم دون السبب، بخلاف العتاق والطلاق؛ إذ هما ليس كذلك، فهما يحتملان التعليق بالشرط، فوجب أن يجعل الشرط داخلاً على أصل السبب دون الحكم، فقياسكم قياس مع الفارق ولهذا أي لأن شرط الخيار داخل على الحكم دون السبب. يحنث لتحقق البيع؛ لأن شرط الخيار لم يدخل على البيع ولم يمنعه عن الانعقاد لا في أحكامه: يعني أن التعليق بالشرط يعدم السبب إلى زمان وجود الشرط، ولا يعدم الحكم كما مرّ تشريحه. صح تعليق إلخ: فيما إذا قال: إن نكحتك فأنت طالق أو إن تملكتك فأنت حر ؛ لأنه لم يوجد قوله: "أنت طالق" و"أنت حر" وقت التعليق حتى يحتاج إلى المحل؛ فإذا وجد النكاح والملك فحينئذ يكون محلاً لورد قوله : أنت طالق وأنت حر، فلا بأس لوقوعه في محله. وبطل التكفير إلخ لأن اليمين لا تنعقد إلا للبر، فكيف تكون سبباً للحنث؟ فلايصح التقديم على السبب وهو الحنث وفرقه بين إلخ بأن المالي يحتمل الفصل بين وجوبه ووجوب أدائه، فلذا صح فيه تقديم الكفارة على الحنث، بخلاف البدني فلا يحتمل الفصل فلاينفك نفس الوجوب فيه عن وجوب الأداء فيكونان معاً بعد الحنث، فلا يصح فيه تقديم الكفارة قبل الحنث فعل الأداء فيكون كالبدني، لا ينفك فيه نفس الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء، فلا يصح فيه أيضاً تقديم الكفارة قبل الحنث كما هو آلة: أي آلة الأداء، فيكون المقصود هو الأداء، وهو إنما يجب بعد الحنث فلا يصح تقديمه على الحنث. وإنما يقصد إلخ: فينفك فيها نفس الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء، فيصح هذا الفرق فيها ولا يصح في حقوق الله تعالى، والكفارة من حقوق الله تعالى، فلايصح هذا الفرق فيها.
مذهبنا.
الأداء،
The invalid aspects:
The Book of Allah Almighty because the option enters upon the ruling not the cause, and therefore if one swore: 'I will not sell', and he sold with a condition [i.e., the option of condition in sale] saying: 'By Allah', the option, he breaks his oath. When it is established that suspension (ta'liq) is an act in the cause by annihilating it until the time the condition exists, not in its rulings, then suspending divorce and emancipation upon ownership is valid, and expiation with wealth before the violation (hanth) is invalid, and the distinction between financial and bodily (acts) is invalid. For the right of Allah Almighty in financial is the act of performance, and wealth is a tool; rather the substance of wealth is intended in the rights of servants.
Like the option of condition in sale enters upon the ruling not the cause. And the explanation of the answer: This means the entry of the condition in emancipation and divorce upon the cause not the ruling, unlike the option of condition in sale enters upon the ruling not the cause which is the sale. For sale can bear prohibition because it is of the type of affirmation, and it cannot bear prohibition because it leads to gambling which is forbidden, and making it connected to a condition is complete prohibition. So analogy would require that sale with a condition is not permissible just as it is not permissible with other conditions, but the Lawgiver permitted it due to the necessity of preventing loss, so it is restricted to its extent, and that is achieved by making it related to the ruling not the cause, unlike emancipation and divorce; for they are not like that, they can bear suspension by condition, so it is necessary that the condition enter upon the original cause not the ruling. So your analogy is an analogy with a difference. And therefore, because the option of condition enters upon the ruling not the cause, he breaks the oath because the sale is actualized; for the option of condition did not enter upon the sale nor prevent it from being concluded nor its rulings. Meaning that suspension by condition annihilates the cause until the time the condition exists, and does not annihilate the ruling as explained earlier. 'Valid suspension etc.': in the case where one says: 'If I marry you then you are divorced' or 'If I possess you then you are free' because his saying 'you are divorced' and 'you are free' did not exist at the time of suspension so that it needs a locus; so when marriage and ownership occur, then it becomes a locus for the occurrence of his statement 'you are divorced' and 'you are free', so there is no problem because it occurs in its proper place. 'And expiation invalid etc.': because the oath is only made for righteousness, so how can it be a cause for hanth? So advance (expiation) before the cause which is hanth is not valid. And his distinction between etc.: by saying that the financial can tolerate separation between its obligation and the obligation of its performance, so advance expiation before hanth is valid; unlike the bodily, it does not tolerate separation, so the essence of obligation does not separate from the obligation of performance, so they occur together after hanth, so it is not valid to advance expiation before hanth. The act of performance becomes like the bodily: the essence of obligation does not separate from the obligation of performance, so it is also not valid to advance expiation before hanth as it is a tool: i.e., a tool of performance. So the intended is performance, and it is only obligatory after hanth, so it is not valid to advance it before hanth. 'And only intended etc.': so in it the essence of obligation separates from the obligation of performance, so this distinction is valid therein, and it is not valid in the rights of Allah Almighty, and expiation is from the rights of Allah Almighty, so this distinction is not valid therein.
Our school.
Performance.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn discussing the rules of ta'liq (suspension) of talaq and itq. It explains how conditions affect the cause (sabab) versus the ruling (hukm). The author clarifies that in khiyar al-shart in sale, the condition enters upon the ruling, not the cause; whereas in ta'liq of divorce and emancipation, the condition enters upon the cause itself. This difference is crucial: sale can bear being rendered permissible despite potential prohibition due to necessity (istihsan), but talaq and itq can bear ta'liq directly because they are not inherently prohibited. The passage then discusses takfir (expiation) before hanth (violation of oath): in mali (financial) acts, one can pay expiation in advance because the obligation of performance can be separated from the essence of obligation; in badani (bodily acts like fasting), no advance is valid because the two are inseparable. The distinction is based on the nature of the right: financial acts focus on performance, while bodily acts focus on the act itself. The passage ends with a statement that this distinction does not apply to the rights of Allah, such as kaffarah.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending a legal effect upon the occurrence of a future condition, where the condition enters upon the cause.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale contract to cancel within a specified period; the condition enters upon the ruling, not the cause.
قياس مع الفارق (qiyas ma' al-fariq): An analogy that fails because the two cases differ in a legally relevant aspect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, why is it valid to suspend divorce on a condition like 'If I marry you'?
A) Because the condition enters upon the ruling, not the cause.
B) Because divorce can bear suspension, unlike sale.
C) Because the cause is annihilated until the condition exists.
D) Because the condition is a necessary precaution.

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq in talaq and itq is valid because the condition enters upon and annihilates the cause, unlike khiyar al-shart in sale which affects only the ruling.

The Hanafi Definition of Al-Amr: A Specific Imperative for Obligation
Defining the term 'amr' as a specific imperative form for demanding an action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of al-amr and distinguish it from opposing views that treat it as homonymous or extend it to actions.

Core Idea
Al-amr in Hanafi usul is the imperative form specifically placed for demanding an action, defaulting to obligation.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi definition of 'al-amr' (command), a cornerstone of usul al-fiqh. The author clarifies that the imperative form is a specific term (khas) with a precise meaning: the demand for an action, which in the absence of contextual clues indicates obligation. The passage then refutes two alternative positions: the Waqifiyya claim that the imperative is homonymous, and the Maliki/Shafi'i view that actions of the Prophet can also be considered commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the author's definition: al-amr is 'a specific word from the conjugations of the verb, placed for a specific meaning, which is demanding the action.' Break it into components: form (sigha), placement (wad'), meaning (talab al-fi'l).
The example 'if'al' exemplifies the imperative pattern.
2
Exclude nouns and particles
The phrase 'from the conjugations of the verb' excludes nouns and particles, which are not derived from verb roots. This narrows 'amr' to only verbal forms.
3
Refute the Waqifiyya claim of homonymy
The Waqifiyya argued that the imperative is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) for obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat. The author refutes this by stating that the imperative is 'a specific word' (lafz khas), not ambiguous.
The refutation is implicit in the definition itself.
4
Refute the Maliki/Shafi'i view that actions are commands
Some Malikis and Shafi'is considered the Prophet's actions as commands (amr), implying obligation. The author counters by emphasizing that 'amr' is placed for a specific meaning (obligation) and its form is the imperative, not actions. Actions are not included in the definition.
The practical effect: a prophetic action alone does not create obligation in Hanafi usul.

Source Text

فصل في الأمر وهو من قبيل الوجه الأول أي الخاص في قسم الصيغة واللغة فإن صيغة الأمر لفظ خاص من تصاريف الفعل، وضع لمعنى خاص وهو طلب الفعل. ولما فرغ المصنّف عن بيان التقسيم الرابع عن الوجوه الفاسدة شرع في بعض أقسام الخاص، وهو الأمر؛ لأن أكثر أبحاث الأصول يتعلّق به. وهو: أي الأمر وهو قولنا: "افعل". من الأقسام العشرين، ومن الأولى في قوله: "من القسم الأول" للتبعيض، والثانية والثالثة للبيان، ويحتمل أن تكونا للتبعيض أيضاً. صيغة الأمر: مثل: "افعل" يصدق عليه تعريف الخاص. تصاريف الفعل خرج به الاسم والحرف الخاص؛ لأنهما ليسا من تصاريف الفعل والتصاريف جمع تصريف، مصدر جمعه باعتبار إرادة الأنواع. وفي قوله : "لفظ خاص" ردّ على قول الواقفية الزاعمين أنه مشترك لفظي بين الوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد. وضع لمعنى إلخ: وفيه ردّ من ذهب من المالكية والشافعية إلى أن صيغة الأمر وإن كانت مختصة بالوجوب، لكن الوجوب ليس مختصاً بها، بل إنه كما يستفاد منها يستفاد من غيرها وهو الفعل، ويسمّى الفعل أمراً كما سميت الصيغة به، حتى قالوا إن أفعال النبي و موجبة كالأوامر، فلما قال: "وضع لمعنى خاص" احترز به منها، فكما أن الأمر مختص بالوجوب كذلك معناه، وهو الوجوب مختص به فقوله: "فإن صيغة الأمر" الخ دليل على أن الأمر
Chapter on Command – It is from the first type, i.e., the specific in terms of form and language. For the imperative form is a specific word from the conjugations of the verb, placed for a specific meaning, which is demanding the action. And when the author finished explaining the fourth division of the invalid faces, he began [discussing] some divisions of the specific, and that is the command, because most discussions of usul relate to it. It, i.e., the command, is our saying: 'Do'. It is from the twenty divisions, and the first [min] in his saying 'from the first division' is for portion, and the second and third are for explanation, and it is possible they are also for portion. The imperative form: like 'Do' – it applies to the definition of the specific. 'Conjugations of the verb' excludes the noun and the specific particle, because they are not from the conjugations of the verb. And 'conjugations' is the plural of 'tasrif' (morphological pattern), a masdar that is pluralized considering [different] types. And in his saying 'a specific word' is a refutation of the assertion of the Waqifiyya who claim it is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) between obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat. 'Placed for a specific meaning' etc.: In it is a refutation of those among the Malikis and Shafi'is who hold that the imperative form, although specific to obligation, is not exclusive to it; rather, just as it is understood from it, it is also understood from other than it, i.e., the action, and the action is called 'amr' just as the form is called by it, such that they said that the actions of the Prophet are obligatory like commands. So when he said 'placed for a specific meaning', he excluded that. So just as the command is specific to obligation, likewise its meaning, i.e., obligation, is specific to it. So his saying 'for the imperative form...' etc. is evidence that command...
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on 'al-amr' (the command) in usul al-fiqh. The author defines al-amr as a specific linguistic form (sigha) from the conjugations (tasarrif) of the verb, placed for a specific meaning – the demand (talab) of an action. He clarifies that the imperative form, such as 'if'al' (do), is a 'specific' (khas) term, falling under the first type of 'al-khas' in terms of form and language. This excludes nouns and particles, which are not conjugations. The author then refutes two groups: (1) the Waqifiyya (a sect that suspends judgment) who claim the imperative is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) between obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat – but it is actually specific to obligation; (2) the Malikis and Shafi'is who argue that while the imperative form indicates obligation, obligation can also be indicated by other things, like the actual action of the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam), which they also call 'amr'. The author counters that the imperative is exclusively placed for obligation, and obligation is exclusively understood from the imperative form, not from actions. The passage is incomplete in this window, but it establishes the Hanafi view that 'amr' is a defined technical term for a specific form with a specific meaning.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form (sighat al-amr) placed for demanding an action, such as 'if'al'.
صيغة الأمر (sighatu al-amr): The morphological form used for command, e.g., 'if'al' (do).
الخاص (al-khas): A word that is specific in meaning, not shared among multiple meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what does the term 'amr' (command) specifically refer to?
A) Any act of commanding
B) The imperative form (صيغة الأمر) that is placed for demanding an action
C) The action of the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam)
D) Any speech that indicates obligation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines al-amr exclusively as the imperative form (sigha) placed for demanding an action, which defaults to obligation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
The Command (Amr) as Literal for Obligation
Definition of amr with proofs and refutations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the command is haqiqah for obligation, identify the three categories of proof (Quran, ijma, language), and distinguish this from opposing views.

Core Idea
The command is literal for obligation unless evidence indicates otherwise.

In this passage from Muntakhab al-Husami, the author establishes the foundational usul principle that a command (amr) in the Shariah is taken as a demand for obligation (wujub) by default. This is the position of the majority of Hanafi jurists and is supported by multiple proofs. The lesson will walk you through the definition, the opposing views, and the evidence, equipping you to apply this principle in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of amr and its default ruling
Locate where the author states that the command necessitates obligation unless there is evidence to the contrary. Note the term 'haqiqah' (literal) and how it applies to 'amr'.
This is the core thesis: amr is haqiqah for wujub.
2
Analyze the three categories of evidence
The author cites Quranic verses, consensus of the ummah, and linguistic usage. For each, understand how it proves that a command indicates obligation.
The verses from Surah Sad (Iblis), Al-Ahzab, and Al-Nur are key.
3
Contrast with opposing views
Several groups hold that amr is literal for recommendation or permission, or that it is ambiguous. Note the schools attributed to these views.
The Hanafi position is defended by the master-slave analogy and the consequence of blame.
4
Apply the principle to derive a ruling
Given a hypothetical command without context (e.g., 'Establish the prayer'), determine the ruling using the Hanafi default.
The ruling is obligation (wajib) unless a contextual indicator (qarīnah) shifts it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
فصل في الأمر
[موجب الأمر]
وموجبه عند الجمهور الإلزام إلا بدليل.
أي الحكم الثابت بالأمر
أي الوجوب
من
هذا
من قبيل الخاص ولا مصادرة فيه؛ لأنه أقام الدليل على إلحاق هذا الفرد بنوعه. ثم بيّن أنه إنما كان النوع؛ لأنه لفظ خاص وضع لمعنى ،خاص كما يقال: الإنسان اسم لنوع من الحيوان الذي صفته كذا وكذا، ثم يقال لفرد معين: هذا داخل في هذا النوع؛ لأنه إنسان كسائر أفراده، كذا في "غاية التحقيق". إلا بدليل يدلّ على غيره، فحينئذ يحمل عليه، وقال بعضهم: هو مشترك بين الوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد اشتراكاً لفظياً كالعين ونقل ذلك عن الأشعري في بعض الروايات عنه، وابن شريح وبعض الشيعة. وقيل: هو مشترك بين المعاني الثلاثة الأول، ثم اختلف فقيل بالاشتراك اللفظي، وقيل بالمعنوي، وهو مذهب المرتضى من الشيعة وقيل هو مشترك بين المعنيين الأولين بالاشتراك اللفظي، وهو منقول عن الشافعي له، وقيل بالمعنوي، فعند هؤلاء لا حكم له بغير القرينة إلا التوقف مع الاعتقاد أن ما أراد به صاحب الشرع فهو حق، كما هو شأن المجمل.
وذهب عامة العلماء من الفقهاء والمتكلمين إلى أنه حقيقة في أحد هذه المعاني عيناً من غير اشتراك ولا إجمال، لكنهم اختلفوا في لعينه، فذهب جمهور الفقهاء وجماعة من المعتزلة كأبي الحسن والجبائي في أحد قوليه إلى أنه حقيقة في الوجوب ومجاز في ما عداه وذهب جماعة من الفقهاء والشافعي في أحد قوليه إلى أنه حقيقة في الندب ومجاز في ما سواه، وذهب طائفة إلى أنه حقيقة في الإباحة، ونقل ذلك عن أصحاب مالك، وتمسك الجمهور القائلون بأنه حقيقة في الوجوب بالكتاب ودلالة الإجماع واللغة، أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى لإبليس: ما منعك أن لا تَسجُدَ إِذْ أَمرتُكَ ، أي ما بقي لك الاختيار بعد أن أمرتك بالسجود، فلم تركته؟ وهذا دليل الوجوب، وإلا لما ذمّه الله تعالى على الترك، وكان لإبليس أن يقول: إنك ما ألزمتني السجود، وقيل: الكتاب قوله تعالى: وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِنٍ وَلَا مُؤْمِنَةٍ إِذَا قَضَى اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ أَمْراً أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ مِنْ أَمْرِهِمْ (الأحزاب:٣٦)، وقيل هو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلْيَحْذَرِ الَّذِينَ يُخَالِفُونَ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ أَنْ تُصِيبَهُمْ فِتْنَةٌ أَوْ يُصِيبَهُمْ عَذَابٌ أَلِيمٌ ﴾ (النور: ٦٣). وأما دلالة الإجماع فهي أن الأمة في كل عصر لم تزل كانوا يرجعون في إنجاب العبادات وغيرها إلى الأوامر، والاستدلال بمطلق الصيغة المجردة عن القرائن على الوجوب، وأما اللغة فهي أن السيّد إذا أمر لمولاه: افعل هذا، فلو لم يفعل يعدّ عاصياً ومستحقاً للعقاب عند أهل اللغة، فلو لم يكن الأمر للوجوب لم يكن ذلك، وأما أدلة باقي الفرق فمذكورة في المطولات.
Book of Allah the Exalted. Chapter on the Command. [What the Command Necessitates] Its necessitation according to the majority is obligation unless there is evidence to the contrary. That is, the ruling proven by the command, i.e., obligation. This is from the category of the specific, and there is no circularity in it because he provided evidence for including this individual in its genus. Then he clarified that it is indeed the genus because it is a specific word placed for a specific meaning, just as it is said: 'man' is a name for a genus of animal whose description is such and such, then it is said for a specific individual: this is included in this genus because he is a man like other individuals. Thus in Ghayat al-Tahqiq. Except if there is evidence indicating otherwise; then it is carried upon that. And some said: it is shared between obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat by homonymity like 'ayn; this is narrated from al-Ash'ari in some narrations from him, and from Ibn Shurayh, and some Shiites. And it was said: it is shared between the first three meanings; then they differed: some said by homonymity, some by equivocity; this is the madhhab of al-Murtada from the Shiites. And it was said: it is shared between the first two meanings by homonymity, narrated from al-Shafi'i in one of his two opinions; and it was said by equivocity. According to these, there is no ruling without a context except suspension with the belief that what the Lawgiver intended by it is true, as is the case with the ambiguous. And the generality of scholars among the jurists and theologians went to it being literal in one of these meanings specifically without sharing or ambiguity, but they differed in which one. So the majority of jurists and a group of the Mu'tazila like Abu al-Hasan and al-Jubba'i in one of his two opinions went to it being literal for obligation and metaphorical for others. And a group of jurists and al-Shafi'i in one of his two opinions went to it being literal for recommendation and metaphorical for others. And a group went to it being literal for permission, narrated from the followers of Malik. And the majority who hold it is literal for obligation adhere to the Book, the evidence of consensus, and language. As for the Book: His statement to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' meaning: you had no choice after I commanded you to prostrate, so why did you leave it? This is evidence of obligation; otherwise Allah would not have blamed him for leaving it, and Iblis could have said: You did not oblige me to prostrate. And it was said: the Book is His statement: 'It is not for a believing man or woman, when Allah and His Messenger have decreed a matter, to have any choice in their matter' (Al-Ahzab:36). And it was said: it is His statement: 'So let those who oppose his command beware lest a trial befall them or a painful punishment' (Al-Nur:63). As for evidence of consensus: the ummah in every age has continuously referred in fulfilling worship and other matters to commands and to infer obligation from the absolute form free from contexts. As for language: if a master commands his slave: 'Do this,' and he does not do it, he is considered disobedient and deserving of punishment among the people of language; if the command were not for obligation, this would not be the case. And the evidence of the remaining groups is mentioned in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh exposition on the nature of the command (amr). The author begins by stating that, according to the majority, the command necessitates obligation unless there is evidence to the contrary. He clarifies that this is a definition of the ruling that the command establishes, namely obligation. He then addresses potential objections by explaining that the reasoning is not circular; it is like classifying an individual under a genus based on shared characteristics. He then presents differing opinions: some view the word 'amr' as homonymous or equivocal among multiple meanings (obligation, recommendation, permission, threat), requiring context to determine intent. The majority of jurists and theologians, however, hold that it is a genuine (haqiqah) expression for a specific meaning, without ambiguity. Among these, the majority of Hanafi jurists and some Mu'tazila affirm it is genuine for obligation and metaphorical for other meanings. Others (including al-Shafi'i in one opinion) hold it is genuine for recommendation. A third group (attributed to Malik's school) holds it is genuine for permission. The Hanafis provide three categories of proof: from the Quran (the verses reprimanding Iblis and affirming no choice after Allah's command), from consensus (the continuous practice of the ummah using commands to infer obligation), and from language (a master's command to a slave is understood as an obligation, carrying the consequence of punishment for non-compliance). The passage thus establishes the Hanafi position that an absolute command (without contextual indication) signals obligation.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): A verbal form used to request an action; according to the majority Hanafis, it is a reality (haqiqah) for obligation
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal obligation established by a command; a binding demand
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal or genuine meaning; the word is used for its original intended sense without metaphor
مجمل (mujmal): A word that is ambiguous and requires explanation; the opposite of mubayyan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a proof cited by the Hanafis for the command indicating obligation?
A) The verse reprimanding Iblis
B) The consensus of the ummah
C) The linguistic usage of master and slave
D) The hadith 'Actions are by intentions'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the command (amr) is literal for obligation unless contextual evidence indicates otherwise, supported by Quran, ijma, and language.

Actual vs Presumed Inability: The Sick and Traveler in Fasting License
Comparison of conditions for license to break fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between actual and presumed inability in the license to break the fast and derive the ruling for the sick and traveler using usul analysis.

Core Idea
License for the sick is actual; for the traveler, presumed.

This lesson compares the legal capacity of a sick person and a traveler to break the fast in Ramadan. While both are eligible for a license (rukhsa), the Hanafi school holds that the nature of the inability—actual versus presumed—determines whether the fast, if performed, counts as the obligatory fast. Understanding this distinction reveals how Hanafi usul al-fiqh uses cause-based reasoning to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note that it contrasts the sick person (marid) with the traveler (musafir) concerning the license to break the fast.
The license is a permission to skip the fast, but the ruling differs when one chooses to fast despite the license.
2
Understand the concept of rukhsa
The license (rukhsa) is a dispensation that allows breaking the fast due to hardship. Its condition (shart) is the existence of a valid cause (sabab).
For the sick, the cause is actual inability; for the traveler, presumed inability.
3
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning
The passage states: when a sick person fasts, the act itself proves he is capable, so the condition for license (actual inability) is absent, making his fast count as Ramadan. For the traveler, fasting does not disprove the presumed inability because the cause (travel) remains, so the license persists.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on causality.
4
Apply the ruling
Extract the fiqh result: if a sick person fasts, it counts as Ramadan; if a traveler fasts, it also counts, but the license remains, allowing him to break if he wishes.
The thamara (practical outcome) is that the traveler may still break the fast even if he started fasting.
5
Derive the usul principle
The reasoning uses the concept of 'ajz haqiqi vs muqaddar and extends by indication (tanbih) to religious need, illustrating how Hanafi usul ties causes to rulings.
This is a key example of ta'lil (causation) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المريض والمسافر: رخصته متعلقة بالصحيح عندنا أنه يقع صومه عن الفرض بكل حال؛ لأن بحقيقة العجز فيظهر بنفس الصوم فوات شرط الرخصة فيلحق بالصحيح، وأما المسافر فيستوجب الرخصة بعجز مقدّر لقيام سببه، وهو السفر، فلا يظهر بنفس الصوم فوات شرط الرخصة، فيتعدّى حينئذ بطريق التنبيه إلى حاجته الدينية.
The difference between the sick person and the traveler: His license is connected to the sound person according to us: his fast falls for the obligatory duty in every case; because by actual inability, the loss of the condition of the license appears by the fasting itself, so he joins the sound person. As for the traveler, he becomes deserving of the license by a presumed inability due to the existence of its cause, which is travel, so the loss of the condition of the license does not appear by the fasting itself, so it then extends by way of indication to his religious need.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis comparing the sick and the traveler regarding the license (rukhsah) to break the fast. The author states the Hanafi position: a sick person's fast counts as the obligatory Ramadan fast regardless, because his actual inability is manifest when he fasts, thus the condition for license (true inability) is absent, so he is like a healthy person. For the traveler, the license is based on a presumed inability due to travel, so fasting does not demonstrate ability; thus the license remains, and by indication (tanbih), this extends to his religious need (i.e., performing the fast is also valid).

Key Terms
عجز حقيقي (ajz haqiqi): Actual physical inability to fast without hardship, as in the sick person.
عجز مقدّر (ajz muqaddar): Presumed inability due to travel, even if the traveler can actually fast.
رخصة (rukhsah): A license or dispensation that permits omitting an obligatory act under certain conditions.
تنبيه (tanbih): Indication or implication; a method of extending a ruling by noting the underlying cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sick person cannot fast at all; the traveler can.
B) The sick person's license is based on actual inability; the traveler's on presumed inability.
C) The traveler's license is permanent; the sick's is temporary.
D) The sick person's fast is always nafil; the traveler's is fard.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes actual from presumed inability, affecting whether fasting removes the license to break.
