دليل اليسر، وإلا فالإمكان ثابت بنفس الزراعة. والخراج بالتمكن إلخ وهذا دليل اليسر حيث لم يتعلّق الخراج برقبة الأرض، سواء حصل له التمكن من الزراعة بنزول المطر وحصول آلات الحرث أو لا. وعلى هذا أي لأجل اشتراط بقاء القدرة الميسرة لبقاء الواجب قلنا: إن الحانث في اليمين الذي كان له قدرة التكفير بالمال إذا ذهب ماله كفر بالصوم. كفر بالصوم لأن الكفارة بالمال تجب بالقدرة الميسرة، فيشترط دوام القدرة على المال لبقاء الواجب، فإذا ذهب ماله انتفت القدرة على المال وبانتفائها انتفى الواجب، فلا يجب بالمال بل بالصوم، وإنما قلنا: "إن الكفارة بالمال تجب بقدرة ميسرة لوجهين: أحدهما ما بينه المصنّف بقوله: لأن التخيير في أنواع التكفير بالمال دليل اليسر، وحاصله أن الله تعالى خيره بين أنواع الكفارة بالمال كالإعتاق والإطعام والكسوة، وذلك تيسير؛ لأن الخيار بين أمور غير متماثلة في المعنى كأنواع الكفارة يتعدّى أثره إلى المعنى فيفيد التيسير لا محالة؛ لأنه إذا ثبت له الخيار يوفق بما هو الأيسر عليه كالمسافر يخير بين الصوم والفطر، ولو كان الواجب عيناً كان أشقّ عليه كالمقيم يجب عليه الصوم خاصة، بخلاف الخيار الذي يكون بين أمور متماثلة في المعاني، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿أَنِ اقْتُلُوا أَنْفُسَكُمْ أَوِ دِيَارِكُمْ (النساء: ٦٦)، فإن أثره يقتصر على الصورة، ولا عبرة بالصورة فيفيد تأكيد الوجوب لا تيسيره، والتخيير في صدقة الفطر حيث خير فيها بين نصف صاع من بر وبين صاع من شعير وغيره من القبيل الثاني، = كتاب الله تعالى ۹۲ بيان صفة حكم الأمر والنقل عنه إلى الصوم للعجز في الحال مع توهّم القدرة فيما يستقبل تيسيراً للأداء. لذهاب المال على التكفير بالمال من قبيل الزكاة، إلا أن المال هنا غير عين فأي مال أصابه من بعد دامت به فكان من أي التكفير بالمال أي في الكفارة أي الحانث أي بعد الحنث وبعد العجز القدرة، ولهذا ساوى الاستهلاك الهلاك هنا لانعدام التعدي على محل مشغول بحق الغير، بعد العجز = فلا يرد أنه يلزم أن تكون هي واجبة بصفة اليسر أيضا للتخيير، وذلك خلاف الواقع. والثاني ما بينه بقوله: والنقل عنه: عطف على اسم "أن" أي ولأن النقل عن التكفير بالمال. تيسيراً للأداء: منصوب على التميز، وهو قائم مقام خبر "أن"، وحاصله أن نقل الحكم "أن"، وحاصله أن نقل الحكم عن التكفير بالمال إلى الصوم لكونه عاجزاً في الحال عن التكفير بالمال مع توهم قدرته عليه في المستقبل تيسير على المكلّف وسهولة الأمر عليه، حيث أمكن له الخروج عن العهدة بالصوم في الحال ولم يعتبر العجز المستدام في العمر في حقه، كما اعتبر في حق الشيخ الفاني وإذا ثبت أنها وجبت بقدرة ميسرة. من قبيل الزكاة في اشتراط بقاء القدرة لبقاء الواجب، فإذا هلك المال يسقط الكفارة بالمال وينتقل الوجوب إلى الصوم ضرورةً، كما يسقط الزكاة بهلاك النصاب غير عين أي غير معين بل الوجوب بمطلق المال. دامت به القدرة: أي ثبت بذلك المال القدرة. وله دفع دخل مقدّر تقريره أن الكفارة بالمال لما كانت من قبيل الزكاة حتى تسقط بهلاك المال مثل الزكاة، فينبغي أن لا يعود وجوب الكفارة بالمال عليه بحصول مال آخر بعد السقوط كما في الزكاة ومع ذلك إنكم تقولون إنه يجب عليه الكفارة بالمال إذا حصل له مال آخر. : تحرير الجواب: أن بينهما فرقاً، أن وهو الزكاة تجب بمال معين، فبعد هلاكه لا يعود الوجوب بحصول مال آخر حتى لا يحول عليه الحول، وأما الكفارة بالمال فلم تتعلق بمال معين بل بمطلق المال؛ لأن المقصود ما يصلح للتقرب الموجب للثواب الساتر لإثم الحنث، فالمال الموجود وقت الحنث والحاصل بعده سواء فيه فأي مال حصل له بعد ذلك ثبت له القدرة على أداء الكفارة بالمال، فلذا تجب عليه. ولهذا ساوى إلخ يعنى لأجل أن المال غير معين في الكفارة بالمال هلاك المال بنفسه واستهلاكه إياه سواء في سقوط وجوب الكفارة بالمال فكما تسقط بالهلاك تسقط بالاستهلاك؛ وذلك لأن المال لما كان غير معين في وجوب الكفارة لم يوجد التعدّي منه على محلّ مشغول بحق الغير، حتى يقال: تجب عليه الكفارة بالمال بالاستهلاك زجراً له على التعدّي، بخلاف الزكاة، فإن المال فيها معيّن لوجوب الزكاة، فإذا استهلك المال لم تسقط ذمته لوجود التعدّي منه على محلّ مشغول بحق الغير، فتجب عليه الزكاة زجراً له على التعدي. عن الزكاة
Book of Allah Almighty. Explanation of the description of the ruling of the matter and the tithe on the destruction of the crop and the land tax if an affliction destroys the crop; because the Lawgiver obligated payment with the attribute of ease. Do you not see that He specified zakat with growing wealth that has a year passed. And the tithe on the crop is real in each one, and the land tax on the ability to cultivate. The difference between facilitated capability and possible capability. And specifically, and upon this we said: The one who breaks an oath, if his wealth goes, he expiates by fasting; because the choice in the types of expiation by wealth, and the tithe on destruction etc. because the tithe was obligatory by facilitated capability; because the possible capability in it was through cultivation itself. So if the condition of keeping nine-tenths for him is stipulated, it is known that it is not obligatory except with the attribute of ease. So if the crop is destroyed entirely or partially, the tithe is voided by its share; because it is a relative name that requires the existence of the remaining shares, an affliction because the land tax was only obligatory by facilitated capability; because it requires the ability to cultivate by rainfall and the existence of plowing tools and so on. So if he does not neglect and sows the land and an affliction destroys the crop, the land tax falls due to the removal of facilitated capability. Then he established facilitated capability in each of zakat, tithe, and land tax by his saying: because the Lawgiver etc. Do you not see that etc., and this is evidence of ease; otherwise zakat could be possible in the wealth itself without the attribute of growth etc. And the tithe on the crop etc., and this is evidence of ease; otherwise possibility is established through cultivation itself. And the land tax on the ability etc., and this is evidence of ease because the land tax is not attached to the land itself, whether he gets the ability to cultivate by rainfall and the availability of plowing tools or not. And upon this, i.e., because of the condition of the persistence of facilitated capability for the persistence of the obligation, we said: The one who breaks an oath who had the ability to expiate by wealth, if his wealth goes, he expiates by fasting. He expiates by fasting because the expiation by wealth is obligatory by facilitated capability, so the continuation of capability on wealth is required for the persistence of the obligation. So if his wealth goes, the capability on wealth is negated, and with its negation the obligation is negated, so it is not obligatory by wealth but by fasting. And we only said: "The expiation by wealth is obligatory by facilitated capability for two reasons: One is what the author explained by his saying: because the choice in the types of expiation by wealth is evidence of ease. Its summary is that Allah Almighty gave him a choice between types of expiation by wealth such as manumission, feeding, and clothing, and that is facilitation; because choice between things not similar in meaning, like the types of expiation, its effect extends to meaning, so it necessarily gives facilitation; because when he has the choice, he is suited to what is easier for him, like the traveler who is given a choice between fasting and breaking fast, whereas if the obligation were specific, it would be harder on him, like the resident for whom fasting is obligatory specifically. Unlike the choice that is between things similar in meanings, as in His saying: 'Kill yourselves or your homes' (Quran 4:66), for its effect is limited to form, and form is not considered, so it gives emphasis to obligation, not its facilitation. And the choice in sadaqat al-fitr where he is given a choice between half a sa' of wheat and a sa' of barley and others is of the second type." Book of Allah Almighty. Explanation of the description of the ruling of the matter, and the transfer to fasting due to inability in the present with the assumption of capability in the future as a facilitation for performance. Due to the going of wealth, the expiation by wealth is similar to zakat, except that wealth here is not specific, so any wealth he obtains afterwards, capability persists with it. So it was from any expiation by wealth, i.e., in expiation, i.e., the one who breaks an oath, i.e., after the oath and after inability, capability. And because of this, consumption equals destruction here due to the absence of transgression on a place occupied by the right of another, after inability. So it cannot be objected that it would be obligatory with the attribute of ease also due to choice, and that contradicts reality. And the second is what he explained by his saying: and the transfer from it: it is conjoined to the name 'an' i.e., and because the transfer from expiation by wealth... facilitation for performance: accusative as specification, and it stands in place of the predicate of 'an'. Its summary is that the transfer of the ruling... its summary is that the transfer of the ruling from expiation by wealth to fasting because he is unable in the present to expiate by wealth with the assumption of capability in the future is a facilitation and ease for the obligated person, because it becomes possible for him to exit the responsibility by fasting in the present, and permanent inability during life is not considered in his case, as it is considered in the case of the decrepit old man. And when it is established that it is obligatory by facilitated capability, it is similar to zakat in the condition of persistence of capability for persistence of obligation. So if the wealth is destroyed, the expiation by wealth falls and the obligation transfers to fasting by necessity, just as zakat falls with the destruction of the nisab. 'Non-specific' i.e., not specific, rather the obligation is by absolute wealth. 'Capability persists with it' i.e., capability is established by that wealth. And he has a rebuttal of a presumed objection: its gist is that since expiation by wealth is similar to zakat such that it falls by destruction of wealth like zakat, then the obligation of expiation by wealth should not return upon him by obtaining other wealth after the fall as in zakat. Yet you say that expiation by wealth is obligatory upon him if he obtains other wealth. The answer: There is a difference between them: Zakat is obligatory on specific wealth, so after its destruction the obligation does not return by obtaining other wealth until a year passes. As for expiation by wealth, it is not attached to specific wealth but to absolute wealth; because what is intended is that which is suitable for drawing near (to Allah) that causes reward that covers the sin of the oath. So the wealth existing at the time of the oath and that obtained afterwards are equal in it. So any wealth he obtains afterwards, he has the capability to perform the expiation by wealth, so it becomes obligatory upon him. And because of this, consumption equals destruction, meaning because wealth is not specific in expiation, the destruction of wealth by itself and his consumption of it are equal in the fall of obligation of expiation by wealth. So just as it falls by destruction, it falls by consumption. This is because when wealth is not specific in the obligation of expiation, there is no transgression from him on a place occupied by the right of another, such that it would be said: expiation by wealth is obligatory upon him by consumption as a deterrent for transgression. Unlike zakat, for wealth in it is specific for the obligation of zakat, so if he consumes the wealth, his liability does not fall due to his transgression on a place occupied by the right of another, so zakat becomes obligatory upon him as a deterrent for transgression. About zakat.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work explains the principle of 'qudra muyassara' (facilitated capability) and its application to various obligations such as zakat, ushr (tithe), kharaj (land tax), and kaffarah (expiation for breaking an oath). The author distinguishes between qudra muyassara (facilitated capability) and qudra mumkina (possible capability). For obligations like ushr and kharaj, the Lawgiver has conditioned them on ease. For example, ushr is only due on the crop after deducting one-tenth for the owner, indicating that it is not obligatory unless the crop is sufficient. If the crop is destroyed entirely or partially, the ushr falls proportionally. Similarly, kharaj is not attached to the land itself but to the ability to cultivate; if the crop is destroyed by an affliction, the tax is dropped. The author then applies this to the case of a person who swears an oath and has the means to expiate by wealth. If his wealth is lost, he must expiate by fasting because the obligation was based on facilitated capability, which has now disappeared. Two proofs are given for this: first, the choice among types of expiation (manumission, feeding, clothing) indicates ease, unlike choices among similar things which emphasize obligation. Second, the transfer from expiation by wealth to fasting due to current inability, with the possibility of future ability, is itself a facilitation. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why does the obligation of expiation by wealth return when new wealth is obtained, whereas zakat does not return after the nisab is destroyed? The answer is that zakat is attached to specific wealth (e.g., a particular flock or crop), so once that specific wealth is gone, the obligation does not revive until a new haul. In contrast, expiation by wealth is attached to absolute wealth; any wealth obtained suffices, so the obligation returns when new wealth is acquired. Additionally, if one consumes the wealth meant for expiation, it does not trigger a new obligation because there is no transgression on a right of another (unlike zakat, where consuming the nisab is a transgression). This highlights the importance of the distinction between specific and non-specific obligations in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
القدرة الميسرة (al-qudratu al-muyassara): القدرة التي لا مشقة فيها وتكون بيسر
القدرة الممكنة (al-qudratu al-mumkina): مجرد إمكان الفعل دون شرط اليسر
التيسير (al-taysir): التخفيف والتسهيل على المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if the crop is totally destroyed before the ushr is paid, what happens to the ushr obligation?
A) It remains due in full.
B) It is voided.
C) It is transferred to the next crop.
D) It is reduced by half.

Key Takeaway
Facilitated capability (qudra muyassara) is the basis for financial obligations; loss of ease voids the obligation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Qudra Maysara vs. Qudra Mumkina: Transfer from Financial Expiation to Fasting
Explaining the transfer from financial expiation to fasting due to inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between qudra maysara and qudra mumkina to determine whether a financial obligation transfers to a bodily one upon loss of wealth.

Core Idea
Ability with ease is required for financial expiation; only possible ability is required for zakat.

In this lesson, we examine a key usul al-fiqh principle: the nature of ability (qudra) determines how an obligation behaves when the means are lost. The Hanafi school distinguishes between ability with ease (qudra maysara) and mere possible ability (qudra mumkina). This distinction shapes rulings on expiation (kaffarah) for oath-breaking and sets it apart from zakat. The passage from our source text explains why financial expiation temporarily transfers to fasting when the obligated person is unable to pay at present but may become able later, and why this differs from zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage discusses the transfer (naql) of the obligation from financial expiation to fasting due to inability at the present moment, while presuming future ability. Note that the condition for transfer is inability with ease (qudra maysara), not absolute impossibility.
The phrase 'مع توهّم القدرة فيما يستقبل' indicates that future ability is presumed, not certain.
2
Understand the basis: financial expiation resembles zakat but differs in wealth specificity
The author states that financial expiation (التكفير بالمال) is from the category of zakat in that ability with ease is initially required. However, the wealth in expiation is 'غير عين' (non-specific), meaning any wealth that facilitates performance suffices, whereas zakat is tied to a specific nisab.
Specific wealth (عين) = designated wealth like the nisab of zakat. Non-specific (غير عين) = any wealth that can be used for expiation.
3
Analyze the effect of loss of wealth: destruction vs. consumption
The passage makes a critical comparison: in expiation, both natural destruction (هلاك) and consumption by the owner (استهلاك) are treated equally—both cause the obligation to drop and transfer to fasting. In zakat, consumption is a transgression because the wealth was already burdened with the right of the poor, so the obligation remains in the form of a debt.
The reason given: 'لانعدام التعدي على محل مشغول بحق الغير' – no transgression on a locus occupied by another's right in expiation.
4
Resolve the anticipated objection regarding return of obligation
An objector says: if expiation is like zakat in dropping when wealth perishes, then acquiring new wealth should not bring back the obligation. The author responds by highlighting the difference: zakat is tied to specific wealth, so after destruction, a new year must pass. Expiation is tied to absolute wealth—any wealth afterward re-establishes ability, so the obligation returns.
This resolution is the core legal nuance: the condition of ability (قدرة) is not the same in both cases.
5
Extract the usul principle: qudra maysara vs. qudra mumkina
From this analysis, we derive that Hanafi usul recognizes two types of ability: qudra maysara (ability with ease) required for obligations like initial expiation, and qudra mumkina (mere possible ability) required for obligations like zakat and hajj. The type of ability determines whether temporary transfer or permanent extinction occurs.
This principle is foundational for understanding how obligations adapt to changing circumstances in Hanafi fiqh.

بيان صفة حكم الأمر والنقل عنه إلى الصوم للعجز في الحال مع توهّم القدرة فيما يستقبل تيسيراً للأداء. لذهاب المال على التكفير بالمال من قبيل الزكاة، إلا أن المال هنا غير عين فأي مال أصابه من بعد دامت به Explanation of the attribute of the ruling of command: and the transfer from it to fasting due to inability at present while presuming ability in the future, as a facilitation for performance. [Due to] loss of wealth regarding financial expiation, it is of the category of zakat, except that here wealth is not specific, so whatever wealth he acquires later, the ability remains with it. So it is from [the category of] financial expiation, i.e., for the oath-breaker, i.e., after breaking the oath and after inability [persists]. Ability: and for this reason, consumption and destruction are equal here due to the absence of transgression upon a locus occupied by the right of another. After inability = so it is not objected that it would be obligatory with the attribute of ease also due to the choice, and that is contrary to reality. Second: what he clarified by his saying: 'And the transfer from it' is a conjunction on the noun 'an' meaning 'and because the transfer from financial expiation'. 'Facilitation for performance' is in the accusative as a tamyiz, and it stands in place of the predicate of 'an', and its summary is that the transfer of the ruling... from financial expiation to fasting because he is unable at present to perform financial expiation while presuming his ability in the future is a facilitation for the obligated person and ease upon him, since it is possible for him to exit liability by fasting at present, and perpetual inability throughout life is not considered in his case, as it is considered in the case of the decrepit old man. And if it is established that it became obligatory with ability of ease, it is of the category of zakat in the condition of the continuation of ability for the continuation of the obligation. So if the wealth perishes, the expiation by wealth drops and the obligation transfers to fasting necessarily, just as zakat drops with the destruction of the nisab. [Wealth is] non-specific, i.e., not designated; rather, the obligation is with absolute wealth. 'Ability continues with it': i.e., ability is established by that wealth. And he refutes an anticipated objection: that since financial expiation is like zakat in dropping with destruction, it should not become due again by acquiring other wealth after dropping, as in zakat, yet you say it becomes due if he acquires other wealth. Resolution: there is a difference between them: zakat is obligatory with specific wealth, so after its destruction the obligation does not return by acquiring other wealth until the year passes; but financial expiation is not tied to specific wealth but to absolute wealth, because the purpose is what is suitable for drawing near [to Allah] that brings reward and covers the sin of the oath. So the wealth present at the time of the oath and that acquired afterward are equal in this regard; so whatever wealth he obtains afterward, ability to perform financial expiation is established, therefore it becomes obligatory upon him. And for this reason, [consumption and destruction are equal] etc., meaning because wealth is not specific in financial expiation, the destruction of wealth by itself and his consumption of it are equal in dropping the obligation of financial expiation: just as it drops by destruction, it drops by consumption. This is because since wealth is not specific in the obligation of expiation, no transgression from him upon a locus occupied by the right of another occurs, such that it would be said: financial expiation becomes obligatory upon him due to consumption as a deterrent for his transgression. Unlike zakat, where the wealth is specific for the obligation of zakat; so if he consumes the wealth, his liability does not drop due to the existence of transgression from him upon a locus occupied by the right of another, so zakat becomes obligatory upon him as a deterrent for the transgression. Regarding zakat.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the nature of ability (qudra) in financial expiation (kaffarah) for a broken oath. The author explains that when a person is unable to pay the expiation financially at the present moment, but it is possible he may become able later, the obligation transfers to fasting temporarily as a facilitation (taysir). The transfer is not permanent; if later he acquires wealth, the financial expiation becomes due again. This is contrasted with zakat, which is tied to specific wealth: if that wealth is destroyed, the obligation is lifted permanently until a new year passes. The reasoning is that financial expiation is for a specific sin (oath-breaking) and the purpose is to seek forgiveness, so any wealth that enables expiation suffices. Moreover, in financial expiation, both natural destruction and consumption by the owner are treated equally in dropping the obligation, because there is no transgression against a right of another (the poor have no specific claim on that wealth before the obligation). In zakat, however, the wealth is considered to be already burdened with the right of the poor, so consumption is a transgression and the obligation remains. This lesson highlights the Hanafi principle that the condition of ability for obligation can be of different types: maysara (ease) and mumkina (possible).

Key Terms
قدرة ميسرة (qudra maysara): Ability with ease, i.e., having sufficient wealth that performing the obligation is easy and not burdensome.
قدرة ممكنة (qudra mumkina): Possible ability, the minimal ability to perform an act without the requirement of ease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens after the obligation of financial expiation drops due to destruction of wealth, if the person later acquires new wealth?
A) The obligation remains permanently transferred to fasting.
B) The financial expiation becomes obligatory again.
C) The obligation is waived entirely.
D) The person must repeat the oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the type of ability required (maysara vs. mumkina) determines whether a financial obligation transfers to a bodily one when the means are lost, with the key factor being whether the wealth is specific or non-specific.

The Hanafi Principle: Possibility, Not Ease, as the Condition for Hajj and Sadaqat al-Fitr
Clarifying the Hanafi usul rule on possible ability for hajj and sadaqat al-fitr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle that hajj and sadaqat al-fitr are conditioned on possible ability (qudra mumkina), apply it to distinguish between qudra mumkina and qudra maysara, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument on the definition of ghina.

Core Idea
Ability for hajj and sadaqat al-fitr is possible ability, not ease – a condition that does not need to persist once the obligation is triggered.

In this lesson, we explore a key Hanafi usul principle: the type of ability (qudra) that triggers obligations like hajj and sadaqat al-fitr is possible ability (qudra mumkina), not ease (yusr). The Hanafi school derives this from the Quranic verse on hajj and the hadith on sadaqa. We will also examine why the condition does not need to continue once the obligation is established, and how this differs from the Shafi'i school's interpretation of ghina for sadaqat al-fitr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two obligations and their common condition
The passage discusses hajj and sadaqat al-fitr. Both are based on possible ability (qudra mumkina). For hajj, the condition is a mount and provisions; for sadaqat al-fitr, it is owning the nisab.
The term 'mumkina' means possible or minimal, contrasting with 'maysara' (ease).
2
Examine the Quranic dalil for hajj
The verse 'man istata'a ilayhi sabila' (3:97) is interpreted as minimal ability: a mount and provisions. The Hanafi school holds that istita'ah is realized by this minimal level, not by luxury.
The verse does not specify luxury; therefore, the condition is possible ability.
3
Analyze the hadith dalil for sadaqat al-fitr
The hadith 'al-sadaqatu ma kana 'an dhahri ghini' (Bukhari and Muslim) is interpreted to mean that sadaqa should come from wealth beyond one's need. The Hanafi school defines this as owning the nisab, not just surplus food for the day.
This interpretation prevents the giver from becoming needy after giving.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
A hypothetical Shafi'i objection argues that ghina means surplus food for the day. The Hanafi response shows that this would lead to hardship: the giver might become poor immediately and have to beg. Thus, the condition is set at nisab to ensure self-sufficiency.
The argument uses the principle of avoiding hardship (raf' al-haraj).
5
Apply the principle: persistence of ability is not required
Once hajj becomes obligatory due to possible ability, it remains obligatory even if that ability is later lost. This is analogous to witnesses in marriage: they are a condition at the time of contract but need not continue.
The condition is for initiation, not continuation.

Source Text

،ومسلم فلا بد من اعتبار صفة الغناء في المكلّف ليصير بواسطته أهلا لإغناء الغير؛ لأن الإغناء من غير الغني لا يتحقق، كما أن التمليك لا يتحقق. فإن قيل: المراد بالغناء هو كونه مالكاً لقوت فاضل عن يومه، ملك هذه = غير المالك من من * وهذا: أي كون المال نامياً.
As for hajj, the condition in it is the [possible] ability of the usual journey with a mount and provisions, and ease is not achieved except with servants, helpers, and many mounts, and that is not a condition by consensus. Therefore, it was not a condition for the continuation of ability: the continuation of possible ability [is not required]. [For] the obligation. Similarly, sadaqat al-fitr was not obligated with the attribute of ease, but with the condition of ability, which is possible ability, i.e., the mentioned condition so that the one described with it becomes eligible for enrichment. Do you not see that it is obligatory with everyday clothes? [This is] a condition, etc.: so that if ability disappears, hajj remains obligatory as it is, and that appears in the case of sin. And [the author's] remark: this is the beginning of an explanation that hajj and likewise sadaqat al-fitr are not obligated with ability of ease but with possible ability. As for hajj, because a little provision and one mount is the minimum by which a person can perform hajj, and this is what is understood from His statement: "Whoever is able to find a way to it" (Quran 3:97); because istita'ah is realized by what we mentioned. As for ease, it is not achieved except with many servants, helpers, and mounts, and that is not a condition by consensus. So if it is established that [hajj] is established with possible ability, then the continuation of this ability is not a condition for the continuation of hajj; because the continuation of possible ability is not a condition for the continuation of the obligation; because it is a pure condition, and its continuation is not stipulated, like witnesses in marriage. So if possible ability for hajj is lost, hajj remains obligatory. As for sadaqat al-fitr, the author explained it by his saying: 'and likewise' etc. 'For enrichment': meaning sadaqat al-fitr is only obligated with possible ability, which is the legal ghina (wealth), which is one's ownership of nisab. And this ghina is not ease; because ease is only achieved with productive wealth so that payment is from surplus, and this is not a condition. Therefore, the passing of a year is not stipulated for it; rather, if one owns the nisab on the night of Fitr, sadaqat al-fitr becomes obligatory upon him. We only made ghina the condition due to his saying عليه: 'Sadaqa is from that which is beyond need' (narrated by Bukhari [no. 5356] and Muslim). So it is necessary to consider the attribute of ghina in the obligated person so that by means of it he becomes eligible to enrich others; because enrichment from one who is not wealthy is not realized, just as ownership is not realized. If it is said: 'The intended meaning of ghina is one's ownership of surplus food for his day, and that is possible ability, and what you say is [ability of] ease; so sadaqa should be obligatory upon everyone who owns surplus food for his day as is the Shafi'i school?' I say: 'If the intended meaning of ghina were what you said, it would involve a reversal of the subject, and the matter would lead to hardship: he gives today this amount to the poor, then becomes needy and begs, and tomorrow he begs from that poor person the same sadaqa. This is uglier; because fulfilling one's own need to avoid begging is more important than fulfilling the need of others. Therefore, we say: in our school, that which is below the nisab has the ruling of non-existence, to the extent that the Law has permitted its owner to take sadaqa.' Then he confirms his statement: 'Sadaqat al-fitr is not obligated with the attribute of ease.' By his saying: 'Do you not see that it: i.e., the obligation, which is sadaqat al-fitr, [is obligatory] with everyday clothes? i.e., with clothes that one wears and uses for wear. So even if he owns these = non-owner from from * And this: i.e., wealth being productive.'
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Hanafi position on the type of ability (qudra) required for hajj and sadaqat al-fitr. For hajj, the Quranic verse "whoever can find a way" (3:97) is interpreted as minimal ability: one mount and provisions enough for the journey. Luxury (yusr) with many servants is not a condition. Importantly, this possible ability does not need to persist; once hajj becomes obligatory, it remains so even if one later loses the means. This is analogous to witnesses in marriage: they are a condition at the time of contract but need not continue. For sadaqat al-fitr, the condition is ghina (wealth), defined as owning the nisab (minimum threshold of wealth, like for zakat but without the year condition). The argument refutes the Shafi'i view that ghina means having surplus food for the day. The Hanafi reasoning is that requiring only surplus food would lead to absurdity: a person might become poor immediately after giving, then have to beg. Thus, ghina in our school means owning nisab. Additionally, the text notes that even everyday clothes count as wealth, but that does not mean the person is obligated for sadaqat al-fitr if he does not own nisab. The lesson emphasizes that the Hanafi school distinguishes between two levels of ability: maysara (ease, for obligations like kaffarah) and mumkina (possibility, for hajj and sadaqat al-fitr).

Key Terms
قدرة ممكنة (qudra mumkina): Possible ability: the minimal ability to perform an act, such as having a mount and provisions for hajj, or owning nisab for sadaqat al-fitr.
غناء (ghina): Wealth/self-sufficiency: legally defined as owning the nisab (minimum threshold of wealth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, hajj and sadaqat al-fitr are obligated upon possible ability (qudra mumkina), defined as minimal means for hajj and owning nisab for sadaqat al-fitr, and this condition does not need to persist.

Good in Themselves and Good Due to Others: The Classification of Commanded Acts
Dividing commanded acts into good in themselves and good due to something else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts good in themselves and acts good due to something else, and apply this classification to specific examples like prayer and faith.

Core Idea
Good in itself: act's goodness intrinsic; good due to other: goodness from external factor.

Having established that commanded acts possess goodness, the author now classifies that goodness into two types. This division is essential for understanding how commands derive their moral and legal weight. The first type, good in itself, is further subdivided, and examples like prayer and faith illustrate the nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
Recognize that commanded acts are divided into two categories: good due to a meaning in their essence (ḥasan li-maʿnan fī ʿaynihi) and good due to a meaning in something else (ḥasan li-maʿnan fī ghayrihi).
This is the foundational taxonomy for understanding legal goodness.
2
Analyze the first category (good in itself)
Notice that the first category is further divided into two subtypes: acts whose goodness lies in their very institution (e.g., prayer) and acts annexed via an intermediary. Focus on the first subtype: acts like prayer are composed of actions and words instituted for glorification (taʿẓīm), which is good in itself.
Glorification is good because it is thanking the Benefactor.
3
Consider the conditions affecting intrinsic goodness
Understand that even acts good in themselves can lose their goodness if performed at an inappropriate time (e.g., disliked times for prayer) or in an inappropriate state (e.g., impurity). This shows that intrinsic goodness is not absolute but conditional.
This is why prayer is not valid during menstruation or at times of prohibition.
4
Contrast with faith
Faith (īmān) is also good in itself but of a higher degree because it never becomes invalid, whereas prayer can be nullified. This illustrates that within the first category, some acts are permanent in goodness.
5
Explain the second subtype (annexed acts)
The second subtype of 'good in itself' refers to acts that are annexed to the first through an intermediary. These acts derive their goodness from being connected to intrinsically good acts, though the text does not elaborate further.
This prepares for understanding how some acts are good by association.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في صفة الحسن للمأمور به المأمور به :نوعان حسن لمعنى في عينه، وحسن لمعنى في غيره والذي حسن لمعنى في أحدهما وهو القسم الأول وثانيهما عينه نوعان ما كان المعنى في وضعه كالصلاة فإنها تتأدّى بأفعال وأقوال وضعت للتعظيم، والتعظيم حسن في نفسه إلا أن يكون في غير حينه أو في حاله. وما التحق بالواسطة بما كان المعنى في وضعه = التعظيم فصل في صفة إلخ ولما أثبت صفة الحسن للمأمور به في آخر الكتاب لم يحتج إلى إثباته هنا فتركه، وشرع في تقسيمه في عينه بأن يكون الحسن في ذات ذلك المأمور به من غير واسطة. وحسن لمعنى إلخ بأن يكون منشأ حسنه هو ذلك الغير، والمأمور به لا دخل له فيه. ما كان المعنى : الذي اتصف به المأمور به بالحسن في وضعه من غير نظر إلى واسطة يعنى النوع الأول من القسم الأول ما يكون الوصف الذي صار به المأمور به في حد ذاته حسناً في غير حينه كالصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة. حاله كالصلاة الحدث والجنابة والحاصل أن الصلاة حسن لمعنى وضع للحسن في نفسه، فإنها أفعال كالركوع والسجود وأقوال كالثناء والتسبيح فحسنها لهذه الأفعال والأقوال، وهذه الأفعال والأقوال حسن في نفسها؛ لأنها تعظيم للرب، والتعظيم حسن في نفسه؛ لأنه شكر المنعم. ومن هذ القسم الإيمان بالله بل هو أعلى درجةً منه؛ لأنه لا يقبل السقوط بحال، بخلاف الصلاة، ولذا قسم بعضهم النوع الأول من القسم الأول على قسمين: الأول ما لا يقبل السقوط كالإيمان والثاني ما يقبل السقوط كالصلاة وما التحق إلخ يعني النوع الثاني ما يكون ملحقاً بما كان المعنى في وضعه أي بالنوع الأول بسبب واسطة.
Section on the quality of good in the commanded act. The commanded act is two types: good due to a meaning in its essence, and good due to a meaning in something else. That which is good due to a meaning in its essence is two types: first, that whose meaning lies in its institution, like prayer, which is performed with actions and words instituted for glorification, and glorification is good in itself, except if it is at an inappropriate time or state. Second, that which is annexed by means of an intermediary to that whose meaning is in its institution. Section on the quality etc.: when he established the quality of good for the commanded act earlier in the book, he did not need to prove it here, so he left it and began its division. Good in essence means the goodness resides in the act itself without intermediary. Good due to a meaning in something else means the source of its goodness is that other, and the commanded act has no role in it. 'What had the meaning' means the first type of the first division: the description by which the commanded act becomes good in itself, except if it is at an inappropriate time, like prayer at disliked times, or in an inappropriate state, like prayer during menstruation or major impurity. The upshot is that prayer is good due to a meaning placed for goodness in itself, for it comprises actions like bowing and prostration and words like praise and glorification. Its goodness is due to these actions and words, and these actions and words are good in themselves because they are glorification of the Lord, and glorification is good in itself because it is thanking the Benefactor. Under this type falls faith in Allah, which is even higher than prayer because it never becomes invalid, unlike prayer. Hence some divided the first type of the first division into two: that which does not accept invalidation, like faith, and that which accepts invalidation, like prayer. 'What is annexed by means of an intermediary' means the second type is annexed to the first type through an intermediary.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition and division of the 'goodness' (ḥusn) of commanded acts. The author states that commanded acts are two types: those good in themselves (li-maʿnan fī ʿaynihi) and those good due to something else (li-maʿnan fī ghayrihi). The first type is further divided into two: acts whose goodness lies in their very institution (e.g., prayer), and acts that are annexed to this type through an intermediary. Prayer is good because its constituent actions and words (bowing, prostration, praise) are inherently good as they are acts of glorification (taʿẓīm) and thanking the Benefactor. However, prayer loses its goodness if performed at the wrong time (disliked times) or in an inappropriate state (impurity). Faith (īmān) is even higher because it never becomes invalid, whereas prayer can be nullified. Thus, within the first type, some scholars distinguish between that which cannot be invalidated (faith) and that which can (prayer). The second type of commanded acts (good due to something else) is not elaborated here but is understood as acts that are good because of an external factor, not due to their own nature.

Key Terms
حسن لمعنى في عينه (ḥasan li-maʿnan fī ʿaynihi): Good due to a meaning in its essence: the act is intrinsically good regardless of external factors (except conditions like time/state).
حسن لمعنى في غيره (ḥasan li-maʿnan fī ghayrihi): Good due to a meaning in something else: the act's goodness comes from an external factor, not its own nature.
التعظيم (al-taʿẓīm): Glorification: the underlying meaning that makes acts like prayer and praise good in themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is prayer considered good in itself?
A) Because it is obligatory
B) Because it consists of actions and words that are glorification of Allah, which is good in itself
C) Because it is performed at specific times
D) Because it requires ablution

Key Takeaway
Goodness in commanded acts is either intrinsic (e.g., prayer due to glorification) or derived; intrinsic goodness can be conditional on proper time and state.

Types of Goodness in Commanded Acts
Defining and dividing goodness of commanded acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of goodness (ḥusn) in commanded acts and explain the Hanafi classification based on whether goodness is intrinsic or extrinsic, and apply the ruling on how obligation is dropped.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, an act's goodness is Lawgiver-determined: either from its own essence or through a divinely-created intermediary.

This passage introduces a foundational classification in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the distinction between acts that are good in themselves (ḥasan li-dhātihi) and those good for another (ḥasan li-ghayrihi). The author further subdivides the first type and explains why acts like zakat are effectively good in themselves despite involving worldly intermediaries. Understanding this taxonomy is crucial for grasping how obligations are established and when they can be dropped.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The passage divides commanded acts into two types based on the source of their goodness. Note the two categories: good for a meaning in itself and good for a meaning in others.
2
Analyze the first type: good for a meaning in itself
This type is subdivided into two: (a) acts where goodness is inherent in their very constitution, like prayer, and (b) acts joined by an intermediary that is divinely created, like zakat, fasting, and hajj.
Contrast the two subtypes: prayer's glorification is immediate; zakat's goodness passes through the poor's need, but that need is created by Allah.
3
Understand the role of the intermediary
For the second subtype, the intermediary (e.g., poverty for zakat) is created by Allah, making the act effectively good in itself. Recognize why these acts are not considered 'good for another' despite the external factor.
The author emphasizes that the intermediaries are 'created by Allah and attributed to Him'.
4
Extract the ruling
The passage states that for both subtypes of acts good in themselves, once obligation is established, it is not dropped except by performing the act or by a specific impediment (e.g., menstruation for prayer).
This contrasts with acts good for another, which may have different rules.
5
Contrast with acts good for another
The final sentence notes that the above ruling differs from acts good for another (e.g., wudu, jihad). Understand that the classification directly affects the legal consequences.
Wudu can be replaced by tayammum; its goodness is extrinsic (linked to the need for purity before prayer).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في صفة الحسن للمأمور به

المأمور به : نوعان حسن لمعنى في عينه، وحسن لمعنى في غيره. والذي حسن لمعنى في عينه قسمان: ما كان المعنى في وضعه كالصلاة فإنها تتأدّى بأفعال وأقوال وضعت للتعظيم، والتعظيم حسن في نفسه إلا أن يكون في غير حينه أو في حاله. وما التحق بالواسطة بما كان المعنى في وضعه كالزكاة والصوم والحج، فإن هذه الأفعال بواسطة حاجة الفقير واشتهاء النفس في الزكاة، في الصوم، وشرف المكان تضمّنت إغناء عباد الله وقهر عدوه، وتعظيم شعائره، فصارت حسنة. في الأول وهو النفس في الثاني هذه الأفعال الثلاثة من العبد للرب - عزّت قدرته - بلا ثالث معنى؛ لكون هذه الوسائط ثابتة بخلق الله تعالى مضافة إليه.

حكم نوعي الحسن لعينه

وحكم هذين النوعين واحد وهو أن الوجوب متى ثبت لا يسقط إلا بفعل الواجب، أو باعتراض ما يسقطه بعينه، كالحيض والنفاس فإن الصلاة يسقط عن الذمة بالحيض والنفاس، وهذا بخلاف ما هو حسن لغيره كالوضوء والجهاد.
Section on the quality of goodness for the commanded act. The commanded act is two types: good for a meaning in itself, and good for a meaning in others. That which is good for a meaning in itself is two divisions: that in which the meaning is in its very constitution, like prayer, for it is performed by acts and words placed for glorification, and glorification is good in itself unless it be at an inappropriate time or state. And that which is joined by an intermediary to that in which the meaning is in its constitution, like zakat, fasting, and hajj, for these acts, through the intermediary of the need of the poor, the desire of the soul (in zakat, in fasting), and the honor of the place (in hajj), comprise enriching God's servants, subduing His enemy, and venerating His rites, so they become good. The first refers to the soul, the second to these three acts from the slave to the Lord—exalted is His power—without a third meaning, because these intermediaries are established by the creation of God, attributed to Him. The ruling of the two types of goodness in itself: the ruling of these two types is one, namely that when obligation is established, it is not dropped except by performing the obligatory act, or by encountering something that drops it in itself, like menstruation and postpartum bleeding, for prayer is dropped from the liability by menstruation and postpartum bleeding, unlike that which is good for another, like wudu and jihad.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'goodness' (ḥusn) of acts commanded by Allah. The author divides commanded acts into two categories based on the source of their goodness: (1) good for a meaning inherent in the act itself, and (2) good for a meaning external to the act. The first category is further divided into (a) acts whose goodness is intrinsic to their very structure—like prayer, which consists of words and actions of glorification, and glorification is inherently good—and (b) acts that are joined by an intermediary to intrinsic goodness, such as zakat, fasting, and hajj. These acts appear to involve worldly concerns (wealth, appetite, travel) but become good because the intermediaries (poverty, evil inclination, sacred space) are created by Allah and directly attributed to Him, making the acts effectively good in themselves. The ruling for these two subtypes is identical: once an obligation is established, it cannot be dropped except by performing the act or by a factor that explicitly removes it (e.g., menstruation drops the prayer). This differs from acts good for another, like wudu or jihad, which may have different rulings.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): A quality of a commanded act that makes it praiseworthy or legally good.
مأمور به (maʾmūr bihi): An act commanded by the Lawgiver, which may be obligatory or recommended.
حسن لمعنى في عينه (ḥasan li-maʿnan fī ʿaynihi): An act that is good due to a meaning inherent in its own essence or in what is effectively its essence.
واسطة (wāsiṭa): An intermediary factor through which an act acquires goodness; in the second subtype, it is created by Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many main types of goodness (ḥusn) are there for commanded acts?
A) One type: good in itself.
B) Two types: good for a meaning in itself and good for a meaning in others.
C) Three types: intrinsic, extrinsic, and intermediate.
D) Four types: wājib, mandūb, mubāḥ, and makrūh.

Key Takeaway
The classification of commanded acts into good in itself (intrinsic or with divinely-created intermediary) and good for another is fundamental to understanding how obligations are established and dropped in Hanafi usul.

Two Types of Acts Good Due to Something Else
Defines two subtypes of hasan li-ghayrihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two subtypes of hasan li-ghayrihi and explain why wudu is good only because it enables prayer, while funeral prayer is good because it directly fulfills the right of the Muslim.

Core Idea
An act can be ruled good not for itself but for what it leads to—either through a subsequent act or by itself.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, commanded acts can be good (hasan) either in themselves or due to something else. This passage dissects the latter category, 'good due to something else' (hasan li-ghayrihi), into two distinct types. The key difference lies in how the intended benefit is achieved: either by a subsequent intentional act (like wudu enabling prayer) or by the act itself (like funeral prayer fulfilling the deceased's right). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly deriving the ruling of such acts—they remain obligatory only as long as their underlying purpose remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes
The author divides hasan li-ghayrihi into two types based on when the benefit (the 'meaning' or 'purpose') is obtained. Read the first few lines to find the criterion.
Type 1: benefit after the act by another act. Type 2: benefit by the act itself.
2
Analyze the first type: wudu and walking to Friday prayer
Why are these acts not good in themselves? What is the benefit they lead to? The passage explains that wudu in itself is mere cleaning and wasting water; its goodness comes from enabling prayer, which is a separate commanded act.
The benefit (prayer) is not achieved by wudu alone; a separate intentional act (salah) is needed.
3
Analyze the second type: funeral prayer, jihad, hudud
For these, the benefit is obtained immediately. For example, funeral prayer in itself resembles innovation but it fulfills the right of the deceased Muslim, and that fulfillment happens through the prayer itself, without needing another act.
Jihad directly achieves exalting Allah's word; hudud directly achieve deterrence.
4
Extract the common ruling
Both types share the same hukm: the obligation of the act continues as long as the underlying purpose remains obligatory, and it ceases when that purpose ceases. The author gives examples: wudu is required only when prayer is required; jihad is required only when the need for exalting Allah's word exists.
This shows the contingent nature of these obligations: they are not absolute but linked to a purpose.
5
Understand the Hanafi nuance on 'wajib'
The passage clarifies that the term 'wajib' here refers to the act established by a cause (sabab), and the obligation is attributed to the command (amr) through the cause. This resolves a potential objection about the obligation being subject to incidental cancellation.
This nuance ensures the attribution to the command is valid, even though the immediate basis is the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حسنه لأجله والجهاد وإقامة الحدود، فإن ما فيه الحسن من قضاء حق وكبت أعداء الله تعالى والزجر عن المعاصي يحصل بنفس الفعل كما في إقامة الحدود كما في الجهاد - فإنه يسقط بسقوط ذلك الغير ويبقى بقائه والمراد بالواجب ما ثبت بالسبب، إلا أن السبب لما عرف بالأمر صحت إضافة ما ثبت به إلى الأمر بواسطته فاندفع ما قيل: إن كان المراد بالواجب ما ثبت في الذمة بالسبب، وهو نفس الوجوب صح قوله باعتراض ما يسقطه بعينه؛ لأنه قد يسقط بعد الوجوب بالحوادث العارضة في الوقت، ولكن إيراده هنا غير مناسب؛ لأنه بصدد بيان حسن ما ثبت بالأمر لا في بيان حسن ما ثبت بالسبب، وإن كان المراد ما ثبت بالأمر وهو وجوب الأداء فلا يصح قوله : أو باعتراض ما يسقطه؛ لأن وجوب الأداء بعد ثبوته لا يسقط بعارض. بعده بفعل مقصود إلخ : يعنى النوع الأول هو ما لا يتأدّى الغير الذي حسن المأمور به لأجله بنفس فعل المأمور به، بل لا بد أن يوجد بعد المأمور به بفعل آخر مقصود كالوضوء والسعي فالوضوء في نفسه تدبير وتنظيف للأعضاء وإضاعة للماء، وإنما صار حسناً لأجل الصلاة، والصلاة لاتحصل بنفس الوضوء بل لا بد لها من فعل آخر قصداً توجـ توجد به الصلاة وكذا السعي إلى الجمعة عبارة عن المشي ونقل الإقدام، وليس في ذاته حسن، وإنما حسن لإقامة الجمعة، وهي لا تحصل بمجرد المشي بل بفعل آخر بعده. وما يحصل المعنى إلخ: يعني النوع الثاني ما يتأدّى الغير الذي حسن المأمور به لأجله بنفس فعل المأمور به، لا بفعل آخر بعده كالصلاة على الميت : فإنها في نفسها بدعة مشابهة بعبادة الأصنام، وإنما حسنت لقضاء حق المسلم وهو يحصل بمجرد الصلاة لا يحتاج إلى فعل آخر بعدها. والجهاد فإنه في نفسه تعذيب عباد الله وتخريب بلاده، وإنما صار حسناً لأجل إعلاء كلمة الله والإعلاء يحصل بمجرد الجهاد لا يحتاج إلى فعل آخر بعده. وإقامة الحدود فإنها في الظاهر تعذيب، وإنما حسنت لزجر الناس عن المعاصي، وهو يحصل بمجرد إقامة الحدود لا يحتاج إلى فعل آخر بعده قضاء حق أي حق المسلم كما في صلاة الجنازة بنفس الفعل كما عرفت آنفا، ولما كانت هذه الوسائط وهي إسلام الميت وكفر الكافر، وهتك حرمة المناهي في النوع بسقوط الغير فيبقى الوضوء والسعي ببقاء الصلاة والجمعة، ويسقط بسقوطهما، وكذا الصلاة على الميت والجهاد وإقامة الحدود تبقى ببقاء حق المسلم وإعلاء كلمة الله وهتك حرمة المناهي، ويسقط بسقوطها كما أشرنا إليه سابقاً، فتأمل.
Chapter on the attribute of good (hasan) for the commanded act. It is the second division. That which is good due to a meaning in something else is of two types: one where the meaning is obtained after it by an intentional act, like wudu and going to Friday prayer; and the second where the meaning is obtained by the commanded act itself, like the funeral prayer. And the second type is that which is good for its sake, like jihad and the establishment of hudud; for the goodness in them—fulfilling a right, suppressing the enemies of Allah, and deterring from sins—is achieved by the act itself, as in the establishment of hudud, as in jihad. For it (the obligation) ceases with the cessation of that other (purpose) and remains with its continued existence. The intended meaning of 'obligatory' (wajib) is that which is established by a cause, except that when the cause is known by the command, the attribution of what is established by it to the command through its intermediary becomes valid. Thus what was said is refuted: If the intended meaning of 'obligatory' is that which is established in the liability (dhimma) by the cause, which is the very obligation, then his statement 'by objecting to what cancels it exactly' would be correct; because it may cease after obligation due to incidental events at the time, but its mention here is inappropriate because he is explaining the goodness of what is established by the command, not explaining the goodness of what is established by the cause. And if the intended meaning is what is established by the command, i.e., the obligation of performance, then his statement 'or by objecting to what cancels it' is not correct; because the obligation of performance after its establishment does not cease by an incidental cause. 'After it by an intentional act, etc.' means the first type is that where the other (purpose) for which the commanded act is good is not achieved by the act itself, but rather must exist after the commanded act by another intentional act, like wudu and going to Friday prayer. Wudu in itself is management and cleaning of limbs and wasting water; it only becomes good for the sake of prayer, and prayer is not achieved by wudu itself, but rather must have another act intended afterwards by which prayer is performed. Similarly, going to Friday prayer is walking and moving feet; in itself it has no goodness; it is only good for establishing Friday prayer, and it is not achieved by mere walking but by another act after it. 'And that which the meaning is obtained, etc.' means the second type is that where the other (purpose) for which the commanded act is good is achieved by the commanded act itself, not by another act after it, like the funeral prayer. For it in itself is an innovation resembling idol worship; it is only good for fulfilling the right of the Muslim, and that is achieved by the mere prayer, not needing another act after it. And jihad: in itself it is torturing Allah's servants and destroying His lands; it only becomes good for the sake of exalting Allah's word, and exaltation is achieved by mere jihad, not needing another act after it. And the establishment of hudud: in appearance it is torture; it is only good for deterring people from sins, and that is achieved by the mere establishment of hudud, not needing another act after it. 'Fulfilling a right' i.e., the right of the Muslim, as in the funeral prayer, by the act itself as you have understood. Since these intermediaries—the Islam of the deceased, the disbelief of the disbeliever, the violation of the sanctity of prohibitions in the second type, and prayer and Friday (prayer) in the first type—are by the act of the servants and their choice, these intermediaries are considered and made included in 'good due to something else.' And the ruling of both these types is also one: the obligation remains by the obligation of the other (purpose), and it ceases, i.e., the ruling, with the cessation of the other. It ceases with the cessation of the other: thus wudu and going (to Friday prayer) remain with the continued existence of prayer and Friday, and cease with their cessation. Similarly, the funeral prayer, jihad, and the establishment of hudud remain with the continued existence of the right of the Muslim, exaltation of Allah's word, and deterrence from prohibitions, and cease with their cessation as we have pointed out earlier. So reflect.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work explaining the categories of 'good' (hasan) in relation to commanded acts. The author first distinguishes between acts that are good in themselves (hasan li-aynihi) and acts that are good due to something else (hasan li-ghayrihi). The focus here is on the latter. For acts good due to something else, there are two subtypes. The first subtype includes acts where the intended good (the purpose) is achieved not by the act itself but by a subsequent intentional act. Examples given are wudu and walking to Friday prayer. Wudu in itself is not intrinsically good; it becomes good because it enables prayer, which is a separate act. Similarly, walking to Friday prayer is not good in itself; it is good because it leads to attending Friday prayer, which is a separate act. The second subtype includes acts where the intended good is achieved by the act itself without need for another act. Examples are the funeral prayer (salat al-janaza), jihad, and hudud punishments. The funeral prayer in itself resembles innovation but becomes good because it fulfills the right of the deceased Muslim, and that fulfillment occurs immediately with the prayer. Jihad involves fighting and destruction but is good because it exalts Allah's word, which is achieved by the jihad itself. Hudud involve corporal punishment but are good because they deter from sins, which is achieved by the punishment itself. The author then discusses the ruling (hukm) of these two types: their obligation remains as long as the underlying purpose (the 'other') remains obligatory, and it ceases when that purpose ceases. For example, wudu remains obligatory as long as prayer is obligatory; if prayer ceases to be obligatory (e.g., for a menstruating woman), wudu is no longer required. Similarly, jihad ceases if the purpose of exalting Allah's word is no longer applicable. The passage emphasizes that these acts are declared good by the Lawgiver due to their connection to a greater good, not due to any intrinsic merit.

Key Terms
الحسن لغيره (al-hasan li-ghayrihi): An act that is good not in itself but because it leads to or enables something else that is good.
الواجب (al-wajib): In this context, the act that is obligatory due to a cause (sabab), but the obligation is attributed to the command (amr) through the intermediary of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Wudu (ablution)
B) Walking to Friday prayer
C) Funeral prayer (salat al-janaza)
D) Fasting

Key Takeaway
Understanding whether an act's benefit is achieved directly or through a subsequent act is central to the Hanafi usul classification of 'good due to something else' and to determining the contingency of its obligation.

Definition and Division of Qabih (Evil) in Prohibition
Defines nahi and divides prohibited acts into types of evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'prohibition' (nahi) and distinguish the four types of evil (qabih) in prohibited acts according to the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
Prohibited acts are evil either in themselves or due to an external factor, determining the possibility of abrogation.

This passage introduces the concept of prohibition (nahi) as the opposite of command (amr). Just as commanded acts are good in themselves or for something else, prohibited acts are evil in themselves or due to something else. The author classifies evil into four categories: evil by convention, evil by revelation, evil due to a necessary attribute, and evil due to an adjacent circumstance. Understanding these categories is essential for usul al-fiqh as it affects rulings on abrogation and obligation.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of nahi
Identify the linguistic meaning (prevention) and the usul technical meaning (a superordinate command 'Do not do'). Note the phrase 'in a superior manner' implies the prohibition is from a superior to an inferior.
This definition is foundational for all subsequent discussions on prohibitions.
2
Recognize the parallel with amr
The passage explicitly states that just as commanded acts are divided into good for itself, good for another, etc., prohibited acts are divided analogously. This symmetry reflects the nature of the Lawgiver: He only commands good and only prohibits evil.
3
Identify the two primary divisions of evil
The prohibited act is either evil in itself (qabih li-aynihi) or evil due to another (qabih li-ghayrihi). The first is further divided into evil by convention (wad'an) and evil by revelation (shar'an). The second is divided into evil due to a necessary attribute (wasf) and evil due to an adjacent circumstance (mujawir).
Memorize the four-fold division: 1) qabih li-aynihi wad'an, 2) qabih li-aynihi shar'an, 3) qabih li-ghayrihi bi-wasf, 4) qabih li-ghayrihi bi-mujawir.
4
Apply the classification with examples
Use the examples given: disbelief (kufr) and frivolity (al-‘abath) are evil in themselves by convention. For other categories, think of acts whose evil depends on context, such as causing harm which might be evil due to the harm, not intrinsically.
The prohibition of acts evil by convention (qabih li-aynihi wad'an) cannot be abrogated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ عن الأمر شرع في مقابله، وهو النهي. فصل في النهي وهو في اللغة: المنع، ومنه النهي للعقل لمنعه عن القبح، وفي اصطلاح الأصوليين هو قول القائل لغيره: "لا تفعل" على سبيل الاستعلاء انقسام الأمر: أي كما ينقسم الأمر في كون المأمور به حسناً إلى الحسن لعينه ولغيره وإلى ما هو ملحق بالحسن لعينه، كذلك النهي ينقسم في كون المنهي عنه قبيحاً إلى كونه قبيحاً لعينه، وإلى ما هو ملحق به، وإلى ما هو قبيح لمعنى في غيره كما ستعلم، وذلك لأن الحكيم كما لا يأمر إلا بالحسن كذلك لا ينهي إلا القبيح. وضبط التقسيم هو أن المنهي عنه إما يكون قبيحاً لعينه بأن يكون ذاته قبيحةً مع قطع النظر عن الأوصاف اللازمة والعوارض المجاورة، وذلك نوعان: الأول ما يكون قبحه وضعاً بأن يكون وضعه للقبح العقلي مع قطع النظر عن ورود الشرع والثاني ما يكون قبحه شرعاً وردّ بقبحه وإلا فيجوّزه العقل، أو لغيره بأن يكون القبح للغير، وبقبحه يكون المنهي عنه قبيحاً. وذلك أيضا نوعان: أحدهما وصف بأن يكون الغير الذي قبح لأجله المنهي وصفاً لازماً للمنهي وثانيهما مجاور بأن يكون الغير الذي قبح لأجله المنهي عنه مجاوراً له في بعض الأحيان، ومنفكا عنه في بعضها، فصار الأقسام أربعة، فالأول ما بينه المصنف بقوله: ماقبح إلخ. كالكفر والعبث : فالكفر قبيح لعينه وضعاً؛ لأن في ذاته قبحاً يعرف بمجرد العقل، وهو كفران المنعم، ولذا لاينسخ حرمته، وكذا العبث فإنه لما كان عبارة عن فعل خال عن الفائدة، أو عما ليس له عاقبة حميدة يعرف قبحه الذي في ذاته بمجرد العقل.
And when he finished with the command, he began with its opposite, which is the prohibition. Chapter on prohibition: Linguistically, it means prevention; from it is the prohibition of the intellect, because it prevents from evil. Technically, according to the usul scholars, it is the saying of a person to another: 'Do not do' in a superior manner. Division of the command: That is, just as the command is divided regarding the commanded act being good into good for itself, good for another, and what is annexed to good for itself, similarly, the prohibition is divided regarding the prohibited act being evil into evil for itself, what is annexed to it, and evil due to a meaning in something else, as you will learn. That is because the Wise One does not command except what is good, nor does He prohibit except what is evil. The regulation of the division is that the prohibited act is either evil for itself, meaning its essence is evil regardless of necessary attributes and accidental circumstances. This is of two types: the first is that its evil is by convention (wad'an), meaning its constitution is for intellectual evil, regardless of the arrival of revelation; the second is that its evil is by revelation (shar'an), but revelation repudiates it with its evil; otherwise, the intellect would permit it. Or (the prohibited act is evil) due to something else, meaning the evil is for another, and by that evil the prohibited act becomes evil. This is also of two types: one is a description (wasf), meaning that the other for which the prohibition is evil is a necessary attribute of the prohibited act; the second is adjacent (mujawir), meaning that the other for which the prohibited act is evil is adjacent to it sometimes and separate from it at other times. Thus the divisions become four. The first is what the author explained by his statement: 'What is evil for itself by convention, like disbelief (kufr) and frivolity (al-'abath).' For disbelief is evil for itself by convention, because in its essence there is an evil known by mere intellect, and it is ingratitude for the Benefactor; therefore its prohibition cannot be abrogated. Similarly, frivolity, because it is an act devoid of benefit or without a praiseworthy consequence; its evil is known by mere intellect.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul work explaining the concept of prohibition (nahi) and the categories of evil (qabih) in prohibited acts. The author begins by defining 'prohibition' linguistically and technically. Technically, it is a command using the phrase 'Do not do' in a superordinate manner. The main point is to divide prohibited acts into types of evil, analogous to the division of commanded acts into types of good. Just as commanded acts are good either in themselves (li-aynihi) or due to something else (li-ghayrihi), prohibited acts are evil either in themselves or due to something else. The passage further divides 'evil for itself' into two subtypes: that which is evil by convention (wad'an), meaning its evil is recognized by pure intellect regardless of revelation (e.g., disbelief and frivolity), and that which is evil by revelation (shar'an), meaning revelation declares it evil but the intellect might not see it as inherently evil (e.g., some prohibitions that are only known through divine law). Next, 'evil due to something else' is divided into two subtypes: one where the external factor is a necessary attribute of the act (e.g., something that is always associated with it), and one where the external factor is sometimes adjacent and sometimes separate (i.e., the act can be evil in some contexts but not others). The author gives examples for the first type: disbelief (kufr) and frivolity (al-'abath) are evil in themselves by convention; their evil is known by reason, and their prohibition is eternal, not subject to abrogation. The passage notes that the Wise Lawgiver only prohibits what is evil, just as He only commands what is good.

Key Terms
النهي (an-nahy): Linguistically: prevention. Technically in usul: the saying 'Do not do' uttered in a superordinate manner.
القبيح لعينه (al-qabih li-aynihi): An act that is evil in its very essence, regardless of external factors. Further divided into that which is evil by convention (intellect alone) and that which is evil by revelation.
القبيح لغيره (al-qabih li-ghayrihi): An act that is evil due to an external factor, either a necessary attribute (wasf) or an adjacent circumstance (mujawir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is an example of an act that is evil in itself by convention (wad'an)?
A) Drinking alcohol
B) Disbelief (kufr)
C) Backbiting
D) Wearing silk for a woman

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi division of evil into four types is essential for understanding the permanence or contingency of prohibitions.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Types of the Prohibited in Hanafi Usul
Explains the two categories of al-manhiyy 'anhu with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two main categories of al-manhiyy 'anhu: al-qabih li-'aynihi and al-qabih li-ghayrihi, and further subcategorize the latter into ma jawarahu al-ma'na jama'an and ma ittasala bihi al-ma'na wasfan.

Core Idea
Prohibition is either inherent in the act itself or due to an external factor that may be separable or inseparable.

This passage introduces the Hanafi classification of prohibited acts (al-manhiyy 'anhu). Understanding this taxonomy is essential because the legal consequence of a prohibition—whether the act is valid, corrupt, or disliked—depends on the type of prohibition. The author first distinguishes between what is inherently evil (qabih li-'aynihi) and what is evil because of something else (qabih li-ghayrihi), then divides the latter into two subtypes based on how the evil factor relates to the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main categories
Recognize the two broad divisions: (1) al-qabih li-'aynihi (inherently evil) and (2) al-qabih li-ghayrihi (evil due to something else). Note that the first category is not further subdivided, while the second has subtypes.
Inherently evil acts are those whose prohibition is known by reason (e.g., lying). The passage focuses on the second category.
2
Define al-qabih li-ghayrihi and its subtypes
The passage splits al-qabih li-ghayrihi into two subtypes: (a) where the evil factor is adjacent but separable (ma jawarahu al-ma'na jama'an), and (b) where the evil factor is an inseparable description (ma ittasala bihi al-ma'na wasfan). Understand the difference: separable factors can exist without the act, while inseparable ones cannot.
Selling during Friday adhan is an example of (a); selling wine (fasid sale) is an example of (b).
3
Analyze the examples for each subtype
For each subtype, the author gives examples: sale during adhan, prayer in usurped land, intercourse during menstruation for the separable type; fasid sale (e.g., with wine as price) and fasting on Yawm al-Nahr for the inseparable type. Examine why each example fits its category.
In separable types, the act itself is good; the prohibition is due to an external factor that can be removed. In inseparable types, the negative factor is built into the act's description.
4
Understand the role of 'illah and mahall
The reasoning (ta'lil) behind these classifications involves the concept of 'illah (effective cause) and mahall (locus). For example, prayer without wudu is prohibited because the person lacks eligibility (ahliyyah), not because prayer is bad. The 'illah is the absence of eligibility.
This helps later in deriving rulings: if the 'illah is separable, the act remains valid despite the prohibition.
